The Definition, Contract, and Ruling of Deposit (Wadi'ah) in Hanafi Fiqh
Commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq on deposits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wadi'ah (deposit), identify its pillar (ijab and qabul) in explicit, implicit, and action forms, list the conditions for validity, and state that the deposit is a trust (amanah) not liable for accidental destruction.

Core Idea
A deposit is a trust: the trustee is not liable for accidental loss, and stipulating liability is void.

In this lesson, we explore the Book of Deposits from the Hanafi tradition. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines the contract, its formation, conditions, and core ruling. Understanding these basics is essential for anyone dealing with safekeeping of property, as it clarifies when a trustee is liable and when not.

Guided Path
1
Define wadi'ah and its linguistic and legal meaning
Identify that wadi'ah is derived from 'wada'a' (to leave) and in Shari'ah means property left with a trustworthy person for preservation. The act of depositing (iida') is empowering another to preserve one's property.
2
Analyse the contract formation: offer and acceptance
Distinguish the four modes of offer (explicit, implicit, by action) and acceptance (explicit, implicit). Examples from the text: placing clothes before a bath-keeper or saying 'Give it to me' when context indicates deposit.
Silence can be implicit acceptance if the item is placed before the trustee.
3
Identify the conditions for a valid deposit
List the conditions: the property must be capable of possession (so not a runaway slave or bird in the air), and the depositor must be competent (contracting party). Also note the requirement that the trustee (mustawda') be capable of preservation.
Minor or restricted slave as depositor has special rulings.
4
State the core ruling: deposit is a trust (amanah)
The deposit is not liable for accidental destruction (halak), whether foreseeable or not. The trustee is only liable if he transgresses or neglects. Stipulating liability on the trustee is invalid, as in the bath-keeper example.
The text distinguishes wadi'ah from amanah: all deposits are trusts, but not all trusts are deposits.
5
Apply the ruling to who may keep the deposit
The depositor may keep it himself or through his household (those living with him). If he entrusts it to an outsider or an untrustworthy person, he may become liable. The household includes wife and capable minor children.
The last part of the text notes an omission by scribes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الترك، وفي الشريعة ما ذكره المصنف قوله : (الإيداع هو تسليط الغير على حفظ يعني صرع صريحاً أو دلالة وإنما قلنا أو دلالة لأن المنقول في المحيط أنه لو انفتق زق رجل فأخذه رجل ثم تركه ولم يكن المالك حاضراً يضمن لأنه لما أخذه فقد التزم حفظه دلالة وإن لم يأخذه ولم يذق منه لا يضمن، وإن كان المالك حاضراً لم يضمن في الوجهين قوله : (والوديعة ما تترك عند الأمين وركنها الإيجاب قولاً صريحاً أو كناية أو فعلاً والقبول من المودع صريحاً أو دلالة في حق وجوب الحفظ وإنما قلنا صريحاً أو كناية ليشمل ما لو قال الرجل أعطني أو قال لرجل في يده ثوب أعطنيه فقال أعطيتك فهذا على الوديعة؛ نص عليه في المحيط. لأن الإعطاء يحتمل الهبة والوديعة والوديعة أدنى وهو متيقن فصار كناية. وإنما قلنا في الإيجاب أو فعلاً ليشمل ما لو وضع ثوبه بين يدي رجل ولم يقل شيئاً فهو إيداع. وإنما قلنا في القبول أو دلالة ليشمل سكوته عند وضعه بين يديه فإنه قبول دلالة حتى لو قال لا أقبل لا يكون مودعاً لأن الدلالة لم توجد ولهذا قال في الخلاصة : لو وضع كتابه عند قوم فذهبوا وتركوه ضمنوا إذا ضاع وإن قاموا واحداً بعد واحد ضمن الأخير لأنه تعين للحفظ أربطها فقال هناك كان إيداعاً ؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان . درهم كتاب الوديعة قوله : (ولم يدن منه قال الرملي في أصله ولم يذق منه فتأمل قوله : (وخير مولى العبد بين دفعه أو فدائه قال الرملي : صورة المسألة أن العبد هو المقتول فكيف يتأتى قوله «وخير المولى الخ»؟ ولعل تضمن بالهلاك وللمودع أن يحفظها بنفسه وبعياله وإن حفظها بغيرهم ضمن إلا أن عنده مع وجوب مكلفاً الحفظ عليه الضمان بعد وقال في الخلاصة في الإجارات في الجنس الرابع في الحمامي لبس ثوباً بمرأى عين الثيابي الحفظ فظن الثيابي أنه ثوبه فإذا هو ثوب الغير ضمن هو الأصح ، وإنما قلنا في حق وجوب لأنها تتم بالإيجاب وحده في حق الأمانة حتى لو قال للغاصب أودعتك المغصوب برئ عن الضمان وإن لم يقبل ؛ كذا في الاختيار. وشرطها كون المال قابلاً لإثبات اليد عليه حتى لو أودع الآبق أو الطير الذي في الهواء والمال الساقط في البحر لا يصح وكون المودع. شرط لوجوب الحفظ عليه حتى لو أودع صبياً فاستهلكها لم يضمن، ولو كان عبداً محجوراً ضمن بعد العتق ؛ كذا في المحيط. ولو كانت الوديعة عبداً فقتله ضمن عاقلة الصبي قيمته وخير مولى العبد بين دفعه أو فدائه وحكمها كون المال أمانة والأداء عند الطلب واستحباب قبولها قوله : (وهي أمانة فلا تضمن بالهلاك) سواء أمكن التحرز عنه أو لا، هلك معها للمودع شيء أو لا. والفرق بين الوديعة والأمانة من وجهين : أحدهما أن الوديعة خاصة بما ذكرناه والأمانة خاصة بما لو وقع في يده شيء من غير قصده بأن هبت الريح بثوب إنسان وألقته في حجر غيره. وحكمهما مختلف في بعض الصور لأن في الوديعة يبرأ عن الضمان إذا عاد إلى الوفاق وفي الأمانة لا يبرأ عن الخلاف. الثاني أن الأمانة علم لما هو غير مضمون فيشمل جميع الصور التي لا ضمان فيها كالعارية والمستأجر والموصى بخدمته في يد الموصى له بها، والوديعة ما وضع بالإيجاب والقبول فكانا متغايرين واختاره صاحب الهداية والنهاية ونقل الأول عن الإمام بدر الدين الكردري وعلم من كلامه أن اشتراط الضمان على الأمين باطل ولهذا لو شرط على الحمامي الضمان إن ضاعت ثيابه كان باطلاً ولا ضمان عليه وهو اختيار الفقيه أبي الليث قال في الخلاصة : وبه يفتى . قوله: (وللمودع أن يحفظها بنفسه وبعياله لأنه يحفظها بما يحفظ به ماله. والمراد بالعيال من يسكن معه حقيقة أو حكماً لا من يمونه فدخل فيهم الزوجة فإن لها أن تدفعها إلى زوجها، وخرج الأخير الذي لا يسكن معه. وإنما قلنا أو حكماً لأنه لو دفعها إلى ولده الصغير وزوجته وهما في محلة والزوج يسكن في محلة أخرى لا يضمن. ولو كان لا يجيء إليهما ولا ينفق عليهما لكن يشترط في الصغير أن يكون قادراً على الحفظ؛ كذا في الخلاصة. ويشترط أن يكون من في عياله أميناً لأنه لو دفع إلى زوجته وهي غير أمينة وهو عالم بذلك أو تركها في بيته الذي فيه ودائع الناس وذهب فضاعت ضمن، كذا في الخلاصة. هنا كلاماً سقط من الكتبة فتأمل. وقد تقدم أن العبد المحجور يضمن بعد العتق ولعل التخيير في صروة ما لو أذن له بالاستيداع فأتلف الوديعة أو يكون المعنى وخير مولى العبد لو كان المودع عبداً فقتل العبد الوديعة إذ ضمانه في الجناية على النفس وتوابعها يكون حالاً مطلقاً.
The Book of Deposits. Deposit (iida') is empowering another to preserve one's property, and the deposited item (wadi'ah) is what is left with a trustee, and it is a trust, so it is not [liable]. (Then commentary:) There is no doubt that it shares with what precedes it in the ruling which is trust. Linguistically it is derived from wada'a meaning leaving; in Shari'ah it is what the author mentioned. His statement: 'Deposit is empowering another to preserve' means explicitly or implicitly. We say 'or implicitly' because it is transmitted in al-Muhit that if a man's wineskin burst and a man took it and then left it while the owner was not present, he is liable because by taking it he implicitly undertook its preservation; if he did not take it and did not taste from it, he is not liable. If the owner was present, he is not liable in both cases. His statement: 'The deposited item is what is left with a trustee, and its pillar is offer (ijab) explicitly or by implication or by action, and acceptance (qabul) from the depositary explicitly or implicitly with regard to the obligation of preservation.' We say explicitly or by implication to include when a man says 'Give me' or says to a man who has a garment in his hand 'Give it to me' and he says 'I give it to you' – this is a deposit; it is stated in al-Muhit. Because giving may imply gift or deposit, and deposit is lower and is certain, so it becomes an implication. And we say in offer 'or by action' to include when he places his garment before a man and says nothing – it is a deposit. And we say in acceptance 'or implicitly' to include his silence when it is placed before him – it is implicit acceptance such that if he says 'I do not accept' he is not a depositary because the implication did not exist. For this reason it is said in al-Khulasa: If he placed his book with people and they went away and left it, they are liable if it is lost; and if they left one after another, the last one is liable because he was designated for preservation, so he is designated for liability. And for this, if he places his clothes in the bathhouse in view of the bath-keeper, it is a deposit even if he does not speak; the bath-keeper is not a depositary as long as the owner is present; if he is absent, the bath-keeper is a depositary. Similarly, if he says to the innkeeper 'Where shall I tie it?' and he says 'There,' it is a deposit; as in Fatawa Qadikhan. (Then a line 'dirham' and repetition of 'Book of Deposits'.) His statement: 'And he did not approach it' – al-Ramli said in his original: 'and did not taste from it' – reflect. His statement: 'And the master of the slave is given the choice between handing him over or ransoming him' – al-Ramli said: The scenario is that the slave is the one killed, so how can his statement 'and the master is given the choice' etc. be? Perhaps... The deposit is liable for destruction, and the depositor may keep it himself or through his household; if he keeps it through others, he is liable unless... (then some broken lines) ... and he said in al-Khulasa on rentals in the fourth type: regarding the bath-keeper who wore a garment in view of the owner for preservation, and the owner thought it was his garment but it was another's garment – he is liable; that is the most correct. And we only say 'with regard to the obligation' because it is completed by the offer alone with regard to trust; such that if he said to a usurper 'I deposit with you the usurped item,' he is freed from liability even if he does not accept; as in al-Ikhtiyar. And its conditions: that the property be capable of possession, so if he deposits a runaway slave or a bird in the air or property fallen into the sea, it is not valid; and that the depositor [be competent] – a condition for the obligation of preservation; so if a minor deposits and consumes it, he is not liable; if it is a restricted slave, he is liable after emancipation; as in al-Muhit. If the deposit is a slave and he kills him, the minor's 'aqila are liable for his value, and the master is given the choice between handing him over or ransoming him. And its ruling: that the property is a trust, and it must be returned on demand, and it is recommended to accept it. His statement: 'And it is a trust, so it is not liable for destruction' – whether it can be guarded against or not, whether something of the depositor is destroyed with it or not. And the difference between deposit (wadi'ah) and trust (amanah) is in two ways: one is that deposit is specific to what we mentioned, while trust is specific to something that comes into one's possession unintentionally, such as when the wind blows a person's garment and casts it into another's lap. Their rulings differ in some cases because in deposit he is freed from liability if he returns to conformity, while in trust he is not freed from liability according to the difference. Second: trust is a term for what is not guaranteed (mamun), so it includes all cases where there is no liability, such as loan (ariyah), hire (musta'jir), the usufruct of whose service is bequeathed in the hand of the legatee; while deposit is what is placed by offer and acceptance; so they are different. This was chosen by the author of al-Hidaya and al-Nihaya, and the first view was narrated from Imam Badr al-Din al-Kardari. And from his words it is understood that stipulating liability upon the trustee is invalid; for this reason, if he stipulates upon the bath-keeper liability if his clothes are lost, it is invalid and there is no liability; this is the choice of al-Faqih Abi al-Layth, and in al-Khulasa it is said: and by this fatwa is given. His statement: 'And the depositor may keep it himself or through his household' because he keeps it with what he keeps his own property. By 'household' is meant those who live with him in reality or by legal fiction, not those he supports. Thus the wife is included, as she may give it to her husband; excluded is the servant who does not live with him. We say 'or by legal fiction' because if he gives it to his minor child and his wife while they are in one locality and he lives in another, he is not liable; even if he does not come to them or spend on them, but it is required that the child be capable of preservation; as in al-Khulasa. And it is required that the one in his household be trustworthy; because if he gives to his wife while she is not trustworthy and he knows that, or leaves it in his house which contains the deposits of people and goes away, and it is lost, he is liable; as in al-Khulasa. Here some text was omitted by the scribes; reflect. And it has preceded that a restricted slave is liable after emancipation, and perhaps the choice is in the case where he was permitted to accept deposits and then destroyed the deposit; or the meaning is that the master is given the choice if the depositor was a slave and the slave killed the deposit, because his liability in crimes against the person and their consequences is immediate absolutely.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, beginning the Book of Deposits. It first defines the terms: iida' (deposit) is empowering another to preserve one's property, and wadi'ah (deposited item) is what is left with a trustworthy person (amin). It explains that deposit is a type of trust (amanah) and shares the ruling of amanah. Linguistically, wadi'ah derives from wada'a (to leave). The author then elaborates on how a deposit is contracted: through offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). Offer can be explicit (e.g., saying 'I deposit with you'), by implication (e.g., saying 'Give me' when the context indicates deposit), or by action (e.g., placing one's garment before someone without speaking). Acceptance can be explicit or implicit (e.g., silence when the item is placed). The commentary provides examples from works like al-Muhit and al-Khulasa, including the case of a burst wineskin, placing clothes in a bathhouse, and tying an animal in an inn. It then discusses conditions for a valid deposit: the property must be capable of possession (so depositing a runaway slave or bird in the air is invalid), and the depositor must be competent (a minor or restricted slave's deposit may have different rulings). It also covers the case of a slave as a deposit and the liability of a minor's 'aqila if the deposit is killed. The core ruling: the deposit is a trust (amanah), so the trustee is not liable for accidental destruction (halak) whether foreseeable or not, and whether or not the trustee's own property is destroyed with it. The passage then distinguishes between wadi'ah and amanah: wadi'ah is specifically created by offer and acceptance, while amanah includes any property coming into one's hands unintentionally (e.g., by wind). Their rulings differ in some cases, especially regarding liability when the trustee returns to conformity. The commentary concludes that stipulating liability on the trustee is invalid, citing the example of a bath-keeper. Finally, it discusses who may keep the deposit: the depositor may keep it himself or through his household (those living with him, including his wife), but if he entrusts it to someone outside his household or to an untrustworthy person, he may be liable. The passage ends with a note that some text is missing from the scribes and a comment on the earlier case of the slave.

Key Terms
وديعة (wadi'ah): The property left with a trustee (amin) for safekeeping, which is a trust (amanah).
إيداع (iida'): The act of depositing; empowering another to preserve one's property.
أمانة (amanah): A trust; property in one's possession that is not liable for accidental loss if kept properly.
مستودع (mustawda'): The depositary; the person with whom the deposit is left.
ضمان (daman): Liability; the obligation to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is a deposit (wadi'ah) considered valid even without explicit words?
A) When the depositor places the item before the trustee and the trustee remains silent.
B) When the depositor speaks but the trustee does not respond.
C) When the trustee explicitly refuses but keeps the item.
D) When the depositor leaves the item in the trustee's house without his knowledge.

Key Takeaway
A deposit (wadi'ah) is a trust contract: the trustee is not liable for accidental destruction, and stipulating liability is invalid.

Definition of Wadi'ah and Its Ruling as a Trust
Defines wadi'ah and contrasts it with amanah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wadi'ah, state its ruling as an amanah, and distinguish it from general amanah using the two differences given by the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Wadi'ah is a trust — no liability for accidental loss, and any stipulation of liability is void.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi definition of the deposit contract (wadi'ah) and its fundamental legal consequence: the deposited property is an amanah, meaning the depositary is not liable for its loss unless he is negligent. The author clarifies the distinction between wadi'ah and the broader category of amanah, and provides the practical example of a bathhouse keeper to illustrate the principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and core ruling
What is wadi'ah? What is its default legal status? (Answer: wadi'ah is a trust, not liable for destruction)
2
Distinguish between wadi'ah and amanah
Two differences: (i) wadi'ah arises from contract (offer+acceptance), amanah can arise unintentionally; (ii) rulings differ in certain scenarios (e.g., returning to owner absolves liability in wadi'ah but not in amanah).
3
Apply the principle: voidness of liability stipulation
The passage states stipulating liability on a trustee is invalid; example of bathhouse keeper. Understand the consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي أمانة فلا تضمن بالهلاك سواء أمكن التحرز عنه أو لا، هلك معها للمودع شيء أو لا. والفرق بين الوديعة والأمانة من وجهين : أحدهما أن الوديعة خاصة بما ذكرناه والأمانة خاصة بما لو وقع في يده شيء من غير قصده بأن هبت الريح بثوب إنسان وألقته في حجر غيره. وحكمهما مختلف في بعض الصور لأن في الوديعة يبرأ عن الضمان إذا عاد إلى الوفاق وفي الأمانة لا يبرأ عن الخلاف. الثاني أن الأمانة علم لما هو غير مضمون فيشمل جميع الصور التي لا ضمان فيها كالعارية والمستأجر والموصى بخدمته في يد الموصى له بها، والوديعة ما وضع بالإيجاب والقبول فكانا متغايرين واختاره صاحب الهداية والنهاية ونقل الأول عن الإمام بدر الدين الكردري وعلم من كلامه أن اشتراط الضمان على الأمين باطل ولهذا لو شرط على الحمامي الضمان إن ضاعت ثيابه كان باطلاً ولا ضمان عليه وهو اختيار الفقيه أبي الليث قال في الخلاصة : وبه يفتى .
And it is a trust, so it is not liable for destruction, whether it is possible to guard against it or not, and whether something of the depositor perishes with it or not. The difference between wadi'ah and amanah is in two respects: one is that wadi'ah is specific to what we have mentioned, and amanah is specific to what comes into one's hand unintentionally, such as when the wind blows someone's garment and casts it into another's lap. Their rulings differ in some cases because in wadi'ah he is absolved of liability if he returns to accordance, while in amanah he is not absolved due to disagreement. The second is that amanah is a term for what is not guaranteed, thus it includes all cases where there is no liability, such as loan for use, hire, and the one bequeathed service in the hand of the legatee, while wadi'ah is what is placed by offer and acceptance, so they are different. The author of Al-Hidayah and Al-Nihayah chose this. The first is transmitted from Imam Badr al-Din al-Kardari, and from his speech it is understood that stipulating liability on the trustee is invalid. Hence, if one stipulates on the bathhouse keeper liability if his clothes are lost, it is invalid and he is not liable. This is the choice of Faqih Abu al-Layth. He said in Al-Khulasa: 'And this is the fatwa.'
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage defines the legal nature of a deposit (wadi'ah) as a trust (amanah). The primary ruling is that the depositary is not liable for the destruction of the deposited item, regardless of whether the destruction was foreseeable or not, and even if the depositor's other property also perished. The author then clarifies the distinction between wadi'ah (a specific contract of deposit) and the broader concept of amanah. Two key differences are given: (1) Wadi'ah arises only through mutual offer and acceptance, whereas general amanah can occur unintentionally, like when the wind blows someone's cloth into another's possession. (2) Their rulings differ in certain scenarios; for example, in wadi'ah, if the depositary returns the item to the owner, he is absolved of future liability unanimously, whereas in general amanah there is a disagreement. The passage further notes that amanah is a term encompassing all non-liable possessions (e.g., borrowed items, hired goods, bequeathed service), while wadi'ah is a subset defined by contract. The chosen position (mufta bihi) is that wadi'ah is a trust, and any condition stipulating liability on the depositary is void. This is illustrated by the example of a bathhouse keeper: if a customer stipulates that the keeper is liable for lost clothes, the condition is invalid and the keeper bears no liability.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): A contract whereby one entrusts his property to another for safekeeping, created by offer and acceptance, rendering the property an amanah.
الأمانة (al-amanah): A thing held without liability for loss, encompassing all cases where property comes into one's hands without guarantee.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability; obligation to compensate for loss or destruction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The stipulation is valid and he is liable.
B) The stipulation is invalid and he is not liable.
C) He is liable regardless of stipulation.
D) The stipulation is valid only if the customer agrees.

Key Takeaway
Wadi'ah is an amanah, so the depositary is not liable for accidental loss, and any stipulation of liability is void.

Conditions for a Valid Deposit and Obligation of Safekeeping
States conditions for deposit validity and obligation of safekeeping

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid deposit contract and identify when safekeeping is obligatory upon the depositary.

Core Idea
A deposit is valid only if the property is physically deliverable; safekeeping is wajib only if the depositary is legally competent.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the key conditions for a valid deposit (wadi'ah) contract. It establishes that the deposited item must be physically capable of being handed over, and that the depositary must be legally competent (mukallaf) for safekeeping to be obligatory. Understanding these conditions prevents common misunderstandings about liability when the depositary is a child or a slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first condition: physical deliverability
The deposited property must be capable of being physically grasped (ithbat al-yad). If it is not deliverable, the deposit is invalid.
Examples: a runaway slave, a bird in the air, or property sunk in the sea.
2
Note the examples of invalid deposits
Each example illustrates a property that cannot be effectively handed over. This shows the scope of the condition.
3
Identify the second condition: legal capacity for obligation
Safekeeping becomes wajib only when the depositary is mukallaf. If the depositary is a child or insane, there is no liability for loss.
The text states: 'if one deposits with a child and he consumes it, he is not liable'.
4
Examine the case of a restricted slave
A restricted slave (abd mahjur) is not liable during restriction, but becomes liable after emancipation. This shows that capacity can change.
Liability arises once the restriction is lifted.
5
Understand the special case of a deposited slave killed by the depositary
If the deposit itself is a slave and the child depositary kills it, the child's 'aqila is liable for the value, and the owner has options.
This illustrates liability arising from harm caused by a non-mukallaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرطها كون المال قابلاً لإثبات اليد عليه حتى لو أودع الآبق أو الطير الذي في الهواء والمال الساقط في البحر لا يصح وكون المودع. شرط لوجوب الحفظ عليه حتى لو أودع صبياً فاستهلكها لم يضمن، ولو كان عبداً محجوراً ضمن بعد العتق ؛ كذا في المحيط. ولو كانت الوديعة عبداً فقتله ضمن عاقلة الصبي قيمته وخير مولى العبد بين دفعه أو فدائه وحكمها كون المال أمانة والأداء عند الطلب واستحباب قبولها
Its condition is that the property be such that hand can be placed upon it; so if one deposits a runaway slave, or a bird in the air, or property that has fallen into the sea, it is not valid. And [the condition of] the depositor is a condition for the obligation of safekeeping upon him; so if one deposits with a child and he consumes [the deposit], he is not liable. And if [the depositor] is a restricted slave, he becomes liable after emancipation. Likewise in Al-Muhit. And if the deposit is a slave and he kills it, the 'aqila of the child is liable for its value, and the master of the slave is given the choice between surrendering him or paying ransom. Its ruling is that the property is a trust, and it must be returned upon demand, and its acceptance is recommended.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage lists the conditions for a valid deposit contract and the obligations arising from it. First, the deposited property must be physically capable of being handed over (qabil li-ithbat al-yad). Hence, depositing a runaway slave, a bird in the air, or property that has fallen into the sea is invalid because such items cannot be effectively delivered. Second, the depositor (or depositary) must be legally competent (mukallaf) for safekeeping to be obligatory. If a child accepts a deposit and then destroys it, he is not liable due to his lack of capacity. However, if a restricted slave (abd m'ahrur) deposits, he becomes liable after emancipation because the restriction then lifts. The passage also addresses a scenario where the deposit itself is a slave and the child depositary kills it: the child's 'aqila (paternal relatives) are liable for the value, and the slave's master has the option to either hand over the child (for retaliation if applicable) or pay ransom (fida'). Finally, the deposit's ruling (hukm) is that it is a trust (amanah) that must be returned upon demand, and accepting a deposit is recommended (mustahabb).

Key Terms
إثبات اليد (ithbat al-yad): The ability to physically take possession of the deposited item.
عاقلة (al-aqila): The paternal relatives of a person liable for paying blood money (diyah) or other compensation in cases of homicide or injury caused by that person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a child destroys a deposit entrusted to him?
A) The child is liable for its value.
B) The child's 'aqila is liable.
C) The child is not liable.
D) The child must be punished.

Key Takeaway
A deposit is valid only if the property is physically deliverable and safekeeping is wajib only on a legally competent depositary.

Depositary Keeping Deposit with Dependents
Rule on depositary's right to keep deposit with dependents and liability conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that a depositary may keep a deposit with his dependents as with his own property, and identify the conditions under which he becomes liable for loss or damage.

Core Idea
A depositary may entrust a deposit to his dependents, but is liable if they are untrustworthy and he knows.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a depositary's right to keep a deposit himself or with his dependents (ayal). It clarifies the definition of dependents, the key condition of trustworthiness, and the specific rules for children and other family members.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
The depositary may keep the deposit himself or with his dependents, as he keeps his own property.
The permissibility is based on analogy to his own property.
2
Define 'dependents' (ayal)
Ayal are those who reside with him literally or legally, not necessarily those he supports. Include wife, children; exclude a brother not residing with him.
Legal residence includes family in a different locality if they are legally considered part of his household.
3
Identify the condition of trustworthiness
The dependent must be trustworthy (amin). Giving the deposit to an untrustworthy dependent with knowledge makes the depositary liable.
Also, a child must be capable of safekeeping.
4
Apply the ruling to liability scenarios
Liability arises when the depositary gives the deposit to an untrustworthy dependent knowing her state, or leaves it in a place with other deposits and goes away.
The passage also touches on the liability of a restricted slave after emancipation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمودع أن يحفظها بنفسه وبعياله لأنه يحفظها بما يحفظ به ماله. والمراد بالعيال من يسكن معه حقيقة أو حكماً لا من يمونه فدخل فيهم الزوجة فإن لها أن تدفعها إلى زوجها، وخرج الأخير الذي لا يسكن معه. وإنما قلنا أو حكماً لأنه لو دفعها إلى ولده الصغير وزوجته وهما في محلة والزوج يسكن في محلة أخرى لا يضمن. ولو كان لا يجيء إليهما ولا ينفق عليهما لكن يشترط في الصغير أن يكون قادراً على الحفظ؛ كذا في الخلاصة. ويشترط أن يكون من في عياله أميناً لأنه لو دفع إلى زوجته وهي غير أمينة وهو عالم بذلك أو تركها في بيته الذي فيه ودائع الناس وذهب فضاعت ضمن، كذا في الخلاصة. وقد تقدم أن العبد المحجور يضمن بعد العتق ولعل التخيير في صروة ما لو أذن له بالاستيداع فأتلف الوديعة أو يكون المعنى وخير مولى العبد لو كان المودع عبداً فقتل العبد الوديعة إذ ضمانه في الجناية على النفس وتوابعها يكون حالاً مطلقاً.
And the depositor may keep it himself and with his dependents, because he keeps it as he keeps his own property. By 'dependents' is meant those who reside with him literally or legally, not those whom he supports. Included among them is the wife, for she may entrust it to her husband. Excluded is the brother who does not reside with him. We said 'legally' because if he gives it to his young child and his wife while they are in one locality and the husband lives in another, he is not liable. And if he does not come to them nor spend on them, but the condition for the child is that he be capable of safekeeping. Likewise in Al-Khulasa. And it is required that those among his dependents be trustworthy; because if he gives it to his wife while she is not trustworthy and he knows that, or he leaves it in his house where people's deposits are and goes away and it is lost, he is liable. Likewise in Al-Khulasa. It has already preceded that the restricted slave becomes liable after emancipation, and perhaps the choice [mentioned earlier] is in the scenario where he was permitted to deposit and then destroyed the deposit, or the meaning is that the master of the slave is given the choice if the depositor is a slave and he kills the deposit (i.e., the deposited slave) because his liability in crimes against life and their accessories is immediately absolute.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage discusses the depositary's right to keep the deposit himself or entrust it to his dependents (ayal). The general principle is that the depositary may keep the deposit in the same manner he keeps his own property, which includes allowing his dependents to have access. 'Dependents' here are defined as those who live with him, either literally (same residence) or legally (e.g., family members living separately due to circumstances, such as a wife and child living in a different locality while the husband works elsewhere). The key condition is that the dependent must be trustworthy (amin). If the depositary gives the deposit to a dependent who is not trustworthy, knowing her untrustworthiness, he becomes liable. For example, if he gives it to his wife while she is untrustworthy and he knows, and it is lost, he is liable. Similarly, if he leaves the deposit in a house where other people's deposits are kept and goes away, resulting in loss, he is liable. For a young child, the additional condition is that the child must be capable of safekeeping (qadir ala al-hifz). The passage also reiterates that a restricted slave becomes liable after emancipation, clarifying that the earlier mention of choice (for the master of the killed slave) likely applies when the slave was permitted to deposit and then destroyed the deposit, or the meaning is that the master is given the choice of handing over the depositary (if he killed the deposit) or paying ransom, as liability for crimes against life is immediate.

Key Terms
العيال (al-ayal): Dependents who reside with the depositary, either physically or legally, such as wife and children.
الأمين (al-amin): A trustworthy person; one who is reliable with the deposit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a depositary liable if he gives the deposit to his wife?
A) Always, because she is his dependent.
B) Only if she is untrustworthy and he knows it.
C) Only if she damages it intentionally.
D) Never, because he is allowed to keep it with his dependents.

Key Takeaway
A depositary is permitted to entrust a deposit to his dependents, but must ensure their trustworthiness; liability attaches only when he knowingly gives it to an untrustworthy dependent.

10 min
Liability of Depositary for Sub-Deposit and Return to Dependents
Ruling on depositary entrusting deposit to others and various scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a depositary becomes liable for sub-depositing the item, distinguish the ruling when returning to the owner's dependents, and apply the conditions for liability in special cases (key, tenant, carrier).

Core Idea
A depositary becomes liable for sub-deposit only if he separates from the item; return to dependents is like return to the owner.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the depositary's liability when he entrusts the deposit to others. The general rule is that sub-deposit without permission is a breach of trust, but liability hinges on whether the depositary separates from the item. Special attention is given to returning the deposit to the owner's dependents, where the reliable position is no liability, unlike the case of a usurper. We will also explore nuanced scenarios involving keys, rented rooms, and hired carriers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule on sub-deposit
The passage opens with the principle: if the depositary keeps the deposit with non-dependents, he is liable (damin). Understand that the depositary is not permitted to sub-deposit; doing so makes him a guarantor.
2
Distinguish liability based on separation
If the depositary sub-deposits and does not separate from the item (e.g., he stays with the second person), neither is liable if it perishes. If he separates, the first depositary (original) is liable under Abu Hanifa, and the second is not. Note also that if the owner later ratifies, the first drops out.
Separation is key: 'فإن فارقه ضمن الأول'
3
Compare return to dependents with usurper
Returning the deposit to the owner's dependents or to his house is legally equivalent to returning to the owner himself—no liability. This is unlike a usurper (ghasib), who is not absolved by returning to dependents. The passage also notes an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Al-Quduri held liability, but the fatwa follows the view of no liability.
The ruling for borrowed items (ariyah) is analogous.
4
Apply the rule to special scenarios
The passage lists several special cases: giving to a woman for safekeeping (no liability unless she takes to spend), placing in another's safe (liability), giving house key to another (no liability), renting a room with separate lock (liability) vs without (no liability), and hired carrier leaving with a third party (no liability). For each, identify the underlying principle: whether effective possession was transferred.
Focus on whether the depositary still retains control or the deposit is placed in a new 'hand' (yad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن حفظها بغيرهم ضمن أي إن حفظها بغير من في عياله ضمن أن المودع لا يودع فإن أودع فهلكت عند الثاني إن لم يفارق الأول لا ضمان على واحد منهما، وإن فارقه ضمن الأول عند أبي حنيفة ولا يضمن الثاني وإن أودع بلا إذن ثم أجاز خرج الأول من البين؛ كذا في الخلاصة. والرد إلى عيال المالك كالرد إلى المالك فلا يكون إيداعاً بخلاف الغاصب إذا رد إلى من في عيال المالك فإنه لا يبرأ؛ كذا قاضيخان. وفي الخلاصة : المودع إذا رد الوديعة إلى منزل المودع أو إلى أحد ممن في عياله فضاعت لا يضمن كما في العارية. وفي رواية القدوري : يضمن بخلاف العارية والفتوى على الأول، وهذا إذا دفع إلى المرأة للحفظ أما إذا أخذت لتنفق على نفسها وهو دفع يضمن . والوضع في حرز غيره من غير استئجار له إيداع حتى يضمن به. وفي مودع غاب عن بيته ودفع مفتاح البيت إلى غيره فلما رجع إلى بيته لم يجد الوديعة لا يضمن، وبدفع المفتاح إلى غيره لم يجعل البيت في يد غيره. ولو أجر بيتاً من داره ودفعها إلى المستأجر إن كان لكل واحد منهما غلق على حدة يضمن، وإن لم يكن وكل منهما يدخل على صاحبه من غير حشمة لا يضمن. ولو استأجر رجلاً ليحمل له شيئاً له حمل ومؤنة إلى بغداد ليوصله إلى رجل فوجد الرجل غائباً فترك الأجير المحمول على يد رجل ليوصلها إلى ذلك الرجل ينبغي أن لا يضمن، فلو وجد الرجل لكنه لم يقبل يدفع إلى القاضي ولو طلب منه القاضي وهو لم يدفع لم يجبر ا هـ .
And if he keeps it with other than them, he is liable; meaning if he keeps it with other than his dependents, he is liable because the depositor does not sub-deposit. If he sub-deposits and it perishes with the second, as long as he did not separate from the first, there is no liability on either. If he separates, the first is liable according to Abu Hanifa, and the second is not liable. And if he sub-deposits without permission and then the owner validates it, the first drops out. Likewise in Al-Khulasa. Return to the dependents of the owner is like return to the owner, so it is not a sub-deposit, unlike the usurper if he returns to the owner's dependents, he is not absolved. Thus in Qadikhan. In Al-Khulasa: If the depositary returns the deposit to the owner's house or to one of his dependents and it is lost, he is not liable, as in a borrowed item. In the narration of Al-Quduri: He is liable, contrary to a borrowed item, and the fatwa is on the first. This is if he gives it to a woman for safekeeping, but if she takes it to spend on herself and it is given, he is liable. Placing it in the safe of another without hiring him is a sub-deposit, so he is liable thereby. In the case of a depositary who left his house and gave the house key to another, and when he returned he did not find the deposit, he is not liable. And by giving the key to another, he does not place the house in the other's hand. And if he rents a room in his house and gives the deposit to the tenant, if each of them has a separate lock, he is liable; if not, and each can enter without restriction, he is not liable. And if he hires a man to carry something heavy to Baghdad to deliver to a man, and he found the man absent, so the hired man left the carried item with a man to deliver it, it should be that he is not liable. If he found the man but he did not accept, he should give it to the judge, and if the judge requests and he does not give, he is not compelled. End.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage covers the liability of the depositary when he entrusts the deposit to someone other than his dependents. The general rule is that the depositary cannot sub-deposit the item; if he does, he becomes liable if the item is lost after he separates from it. Under Abu Hanifa's view, if the depositary sub-deposits and separates from it, the first depositary (the original) is liable, not the second. If the owner later ratifies the sub-deposit, the original depositary drops out of liability. Returning the deposit to the owner's dependents or to his house is equivalent to returning it to the owner, so no liability arises. However, a usurper (ghasib) who returns to the owner's dependents is not absolved. Two narrations are mentioned: according to Al-Quduri, returning to dependents results in liability, but the fatwa follows the view that there is no liability, as in the case of a borrowed item. Special cases are then discussed: if the depositary gives the deposit to a woman for safekeeping, he is not liable; but if she takes it to spend on herself, he becomes liable. Placing the deposit in another's safe without hiring is considered a sub-deposit, making the depositary liable. If the depositary leaves his house and gives the key to another, but the deposit is lost, he is not liable because he did not effectively transfer possession. If he rents a room in his house and gives the deposit to the tenant, the ruling depends on the degree of separation: if each has a separate lock, he is liable; if they can enter freely, he is not. Finally, if a hired carrier does not find the recipient and leaves the item with someone else, he should not be liable; if the recipient is present but refuses, the carrier should give it to the judge, and cannot be compelled if the judge requests but he does not give.

Key Terms
إيداع (ida'): Sub-deposit; entrusting the deposit to another person.
غاصب (ghasib): Usurper; one who takes property unlawfully.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a depositary gives the key to his house to a neighbor while he is away, and the deposit is lost, what is the ruling?
A) The depositary is liable because he gave the key to another.
B) The depositary is not liable because he did not effectively transfer possession.
C) The neighbor is liable as the new custodian.
D) The depositary is liable only if the neighbor was not a dependent.

Key Takeaway
A depositary's liability for sub-deposit hinges on separation; return to the owner's dependents does not create liability per the mufta bihi.

The Exception for Emergency Sub-Deposit (Fire/Flood)
Commentary on the exception clause from Kanz al-Daqa'iq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a depositary may sub-deposit without liability, and identify the evidentiary requirement for the claim of necessity.

Core Idea
Sub-deposit without permission is a breach — only genuine, overwhelming danger with no alternative removes liability.

In this passage, al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the exception in Kanz al-Daqa'iq that permits a depositary to sub-deposit when there is imminent danger such as fire or flood. The commentary clarifies the precise conditions: the danger must be overwhelming, the depositary must have no household members to help, and the claim must be proven with evidence. This lesson unpacks these conditions and their proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling and the exception
The base ruling is that sub-deposit without owner's permission is prohibited and makes the depositary liable. The exception is stated with 'except' (illa). Note the general principle: the depositary is an ameen and must not delegate without necessity.
2
Analyze the first condition: genuine overwhelming danger
The danger must be overwhelming and directly threatening the deposit. Fire must encompass the house; flood must be imminent. If the fire is not encompassing, the depositary remains liable.
The passage uses 'ya'if al-haraqa' meaning fear of burning, but clarifies that the fire must be ghalib (overwhelming) and muhit (encompassing).
3
Analyze the second condition: no capable household members
If the depositary has household members who could preserve the deposit, he must use them. The example of a dying woman with no family present illustrates the condition.
This prevents abuse of the exception by those who have alternatives.
4
Examine the evidentiary requirement
The depositary's claim of necessity is not accepted without evidence (bayyinah). He bears the burden of proof because the default is liability. This is like claiming permission for sub-deposit.
Without evidence, the depositary is liable even if the danger was real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إلا) أن يخاف الحرق أو الغرق فيسلمها إلى جاره أو فلك آخر لأن هذا تعين حفظاً فلا يضمن به ولهذا قال في الخلاصة: امرأة حضرتها الوفاة وعندها وديعة فدفعتها إلى جارة لها فهلكت عندها إن لم يكن وقت وفاتها بحضرتها أحد من عيالها لا تضمن ا هـ . لأنه تعين طريقاً للحفظ ولهذا قالوا أيضاً : لو أمكنه أن يحفظها في وقت الحرق والغرق بعياله فدفعها لأجنبي ضمن وفي قوله «وسلمها إلى فلك آخر» إشارة إلى أنه لو ألقاها في سفينة أخرى وهلكت قبل أن تستقر فيها بأن وقعت في البحر ابتداء أو بالتدرج يضمن لأن الإتلاف حصل بفعله. وأشار بقوله «إلا أن يخاف الحرق» إلى أن الحريق لا بد أن يكون غالباً محيطاً بمنزل المودع ، وأما إذا لم يكن محيطاً يضمن بالدفع إلى الأجنبي؛ كذا في الخلاصة لأنه لا يخاف عليها في هذه الصورة. وفي الهداية: ولا يصدق على ذلك إلا ببينة لأنه يدعي ضرورة مسقطة للضمان بعد تحقق السبب فصار كما إذا ادعى الإذن في الإيداع ا هـ . وفي الخلاصة : إذا علم أنه وقع الحريق في بيته قبل قوله وإلا وفي الفوائد التاجية : فلو أودعها وهلكت فقال المالك هلكت عند الثاني وقال بل رده إلي وهلكت عندي لا يصدق لأن إيداع الغير موجب للضمان بخلاف ما لو غصب من المودع وهلكت فأراد المالك أن يضمن الغاصب فقال المودع قد رده إلي فهلكت عندي وقال لا بل هلكت عنده فالقول قول المودع لأنه أمين ا هـ . فلا اهـ.
He said: (Except) that he fears burning or drowning, so he delivers it to his neighbor or another ship. Because this is determined as preservation, so he is not liable by it. For this reason, it is stated in al-Khulasa: A woman who is at death's door and has a deposit, and she gives it to her neighbor, and it is lost with her, if at the time of her death there was no one from her household present, she is not liable. Because it is determined as a way of preservation. For this reason, they also said: If he could have preserved it at the time of fire or flood with his household, but gave it to a stranger, he is liable. And in his statement "and delivers it to another ship" there is an indication that if he threw it into another ship and it was lost before settling in it, by falling into the sea initially or gradually, he is liable because the destruction occurred by his action. And he indicated by his statement "except that he fears burning" that the fire must be overwhelming, encompassing the depositary's house. But if it is not encompassing, he is liable by giving to a stranger; as in al-Khulasa, because he does not fear for it in this situation. And in al-Hidayah: He is not believed in that except with evidence, because he is claiming a necessity that removes liability after the cause is established, so it becomes like if he claimed permission for the sub-deposit. And in al-Khulasa: If it is known that the fire occurred in his house, his statement is not accepted... And in al-Fawa'id al-Tajiyya: If he sub-deposited it and it was lost, and the owner said it was lost with the second, and he (the depositary) said rather he returned it to me and it was lost with me, he is not believed because sub-depositing to another necessitates liability. Unlike if it was usurped from the depositary and lost, and the owner wants to hold the usurper liable, and the depositary said he returned it to me and it was lost with me, and he (the usurper) said no, it was lost with him, then the statement is that of the depositary because he is a trustee. End.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the exception clause in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The base ruling is that the depositary (al-muda') cannot sub-deposit the trust unless there is an emergency like fire or flood. The commentary clarifies that the emergency must be genuine: the fire must be overwhelming and encompassing the house, and the depositary must have no alternative—if he could have preserved it with his own household, he is liable. The example of a dying woman handing the deposit to a neighbor illustrates that if no family is present, the act is considered a necessary preservation. The commentary also details that throwing the deposit into another ship and it being lost before settling makes him liable because his action caused the loss. An important procedural point: the depositary's claim of emergency is not accepted without evidence (bayyinah), as it is a claim of necessity that negates the default liability. The final part discusses a dispute between the depositary and the owner about whether the deposit was lost with the second or returned; the depositary is not believed if he sub-deposited, but if the deposit was usurped, the depositary's statement about return is accepted because he is an ameen (trustee). This passage illustrates Hanafi precision in balancing the duty of preservation with liability rules.

Key Terms
إيداع (ida'): Sub-deposit: the act of the depositary handing the deposited property to another person for safekeeping.
عين ('ayn): Determined as preservation: when the situation forces a specific action to protect the deposit.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence: required to prove the claim of necessity for sub-deposit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does a depositary not become liable when he hands over the deposit to a stranger due to fear of fire?
A) If the fire is in the neighborhood
B) If he fears fire and has no one from his household to preserve it
C) If the owner is absent
D) If the stranger is a relative

Key Takeaway
Sub-deposit without permission is a breach of trust; only genuine, overwhelming danger with no alternative removes liability, and the depositary must prove the necessity with evidence.

Ten Holders Who Cannot Transfer Their Interest
List and commentary on ten types of property holders who cannot transfer their interest to another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ten types of holders who cannot transfer the property in their possession and explain the Hanafi principle that prohibits such transfers.

Core Idea
A holder with limited rights cannot transfer those rights – only the owner may create new rights over his property.

This passage from Fatawa Qadikhan lists ten contractual relationships where the holder is prohibited from transferring the property to another. Understanding this list clarifies the Hanafi principle that possession without ownership does not grant the power to delegate or dispose. The commentary from al-Khulasa and al-Tajrid adds nuance, especially regarding the lessee's exceptional permission to sub-lease.

Guided Path
1
Extract the general rule
What common legal restriction applies to all ten holders? They all lack full ownership and cannot create new rights in the property without the owner's consent.
2
Catalog the nine explicitly mentioned holders
List each holder from the text and state the specific transfer they are prohibited from making (e.g., mortgagee cannot re-pledge, depositary cannot sub-deposit, agent cannot sub-delegate).
3
Identify the tenth holder
The text notes the tenth item is missing; from al-Ramli's commentary, it is the musaqi (irrigator) who cannot sub-contract irrigation.
4
Apply the exception
Which holder is permitted to transfer? The lessee may sub-lease, lend, and deposit. Why? Because the lessee has a stronger right to the usufruct.
5
Determine the legal consequence of violation
If a holder transfers without permission, he becomes a transgressor (mut'addi) and is liable for loss, but the original contract (e.g., pawn) is not invalidated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : عشرة أشياء إذا ملكها إنسان ليس له أن يملك غيره لا قبل القبض ولا بعده المرتهن لا يملك أن يرهن والمودع لا يملك الإيداع، والوكيل بالبيع لا يملك أن يوكل غيره، ومستأجر الدابة أو الثوب لا يؤجر غيره، والمستعير يعير غيره، والمزارع لا يدفع الأرض مزارعة إلى غيره، والمضارب لا يضارب، والمستبضع لا يملك الإبضاع، والمستبضع لا يملك الإيداع، ولم يذكر العاشر. وفي الخلاصة : والوديعة لا تودع ولا تعار ولا تؤجر ولا ترهن وإن فعل شيئاً منها ضمن، والمستأجر يؤجر ويعار ويودع، ولم يذكر حكم الرهن وينبغي أن يرهن. وفي التجريد : وليس للمرتهن أن يتصرف بشيء في الرهن غير الإمساك لا ولا يؤجر ولا ولا يلبس ولا يستخدم فإن فعل كان متعدياً ولا يبطل الرهن.
And in the Fatawa of Qadikhan: Ten things which when a person possesses, he does not have the right to give possession to another, neither before nor after taking possession. The mortgagee cannot re-pledge, the depositary cannot sub-deposit, the agent for sale cannot appoint another agent, the lessee of an animal or garment cannot sub-lease, the borrower cannot lend, the sharecropper cannot give the land to another on sharecropping, the mudarib cannot enter into a new mudaraba, the one given an investment for trade (mustabdi') cannot give it to another for trade, the mustabdi' cannot deposit, and the tenth was not mentioned. And in al-Khulasa: The deposit is not to be sub-deposited, lent, rented, or pawned; if he does any of these, he is liable. The lessee may rent out, lend, and deposit. And he did not mention the ruling of pawning, and it is appropriate that he may pawn. And in al-Tajrid: The mortgagee has no right to dispose of the pawn in any way other than holding it; he may not rent it out, not wear it, not use it; if he does, he is a transgressor, and the pawn is not invalidated.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage presents a list from Fatawa Qadikhan of ten types of contractual relationships where the holder cannot transfer the property to another without the owner's permission. The list includes: the mortgagee (cannot re-pledge), the depositary (cannot sub-deposit), the agent for sale (cannot sub-delegate), the lessee (cannot sub-lease), the borrower (cannot lend), the sharecropper (cannot sub-sharecrop), the mudarib (cannot enter a new mudaraba), and the mustabdi' (cannot appoint another mustabdi' or sub-deposit). The tenth item is mentioned as missing, but al-Ramli notes it is the musaqi (irrigator) who cannot sub-contract irrigation. The commentary from al-Khulasa adds general rules: the deposit cannot be used in any way by the depositary; if he does, he is liable. Interestingly, the lessee is allowed to sub-lease, lend, and deposit—showing a difference because the lessee has a stronger right of use. Al-Tajrid clarifies that the mortgagee can only hold the pawn; any use is a transgression but does not invalidate the pawn. This list illustrates the principle that holders of limited rights cannot transfer those rights without the owner's consent, safeguarding the original owner's property.

Key Terms
مرتهن (murtahin): Mortgagee: the person who holds a pawn (rahn) as security for a debt.
مستبضع (mustabdi'): One who is given capital or goods for trade as a mudaraba or similar contract; he cannot give it to another for trade or deposit.
مضارب (mudarib): The managing partner in a mudaraba contract; he cannot enter into a new mudaraba with the same capital without the owner's permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following holders is permitted to sub-lease the property according to the Hanafi school?
A) The mortgagee
B) The depositary
C) The lessee
D) The borrower (musta'ir)

Key Takeaway
Holders of property with limited rights—like mortgagees, depositors, agents—cannot transfer their interest; only the owner may authorize such transfers.

8 min
Liability for Unauthorized Disposition of the Deposit
Commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq on unauthorized acts by depositary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which unauthorized acts (mixing, lending, re-depositing) trigger liability (daman) for the depositary and state the underlying principle that the deposit remains an amanah.

Core Idea
Unauthorized disposition converts the depositary from an amin (trustee) to a damin (guarantor).

The deposit (wadi‘ah) is a trust (amanah). The depositary must preserve it and return it on demand. If he takes any action without the owner's permission that changes the nature of the trust or risks loss, he becomes liable for the value. This lesson examines the specific acts that trigger liability and the rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the depositary's default status
Begin by recalling that the depositary is an amin (trustee) – not liable for loss without negligence or transgression. Any unauthorized use is a transgression (ta‘addi).
This is the baseline: no liability unless he breaks the trust.
2
List the unauthorized acts mentioned in the text
The text specifies: mixing the deposit with his own property such that it becomes indistinguishable, selling, lending, re-depositing, and similar dispositions. Identify each and note that they all involve changing the deposit's status.
Acts like renting out or mortgaging are also included by analogy.
3
Apply the rule: liability arises from any disposition that removes the deposit from its trusted state
The common thread: any act that transfers possession or alters the deposit's identity without consent makes the depositary a guarantor. If the deposit is lost thereafter, he must compensate.
This is based on the maxim that the trust is broken once the depositary acts as owner.
4
Distinguish: mixing without his action vs. with his action
If the mixing occurs accidentally (e.g., water seeps from his property into the deposit), he is not liable because he did not cause it. But if he deliberately mixes, he becomes liable even if the goods are still distinguishable? The text says 'until it is not distinguishable' – so if still distinguishable, is he liable? The commentary implies liability only when indistinguishable. Clarify: Hanafi position is that if he mixes with permission or if still distinguishable, he remains amin. But if he mixes without permission and it becomes indistinguishable, he becomes guarantor.
The key is whether the deposit remains identifiable and accessible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تسليمها فمنعها أو خلطها بماله بغير الإذن حتى لا تتميز ضمنها وإن اختلط بلا فعله لا، يبيع. يعير يعير غيره ما يختلف بالمستعمل والمزارع لا يدفع الأرض مزارعة إلى غيره، والمضارب لا يضارب، والمستبضع لا يملك الإبضاع، والمستبضع لا يملك الإيداع، ولم يذكر العاشر. وفي الخلاصة : والوديعة لا تودع ولا تعار ولا تؤجر ولا ترهن وإن فعل شيئاً منها ضمن، والمستأجر يؤجر ويعار ويودع، ولم يذكر حكم الرهن وينبغي أن يرهن. وفي التجريد : وليس للمرتهن أن يتصرف بشيء في الرهن غير الإمساك لا ولا يؤجر ولا ولا يلبس ولا يستخدم فإن فعل كان متعدياً ولا يبطل الرهن قوله : (إلا) أن يخاف الحرق أو الغرق فيسلمها إلى جاره أو فلك آخر لأن هذا تعين حفظاً فلا يضمن به ولهذا قال في الخلاصة: امرأة حضرتها الوفاة وعندها وديعة فدفعتها إلى جارة لها فهلكت عندها إن لم يكن وقت وفاتها بحضرتها أحد من عيالها لا تضمن ا هـ . لأنه تعين طريقاً للحفظ ولهذا قالوا أيضاً : لو أمكنه أن يحفظها في وقت الحرق والغرق بعياله فدفعها لأجنبي ضمن وفي قوله «وسلمها إلى فلك آخر» إشارة إلى أنه لو ألقاها في سفينة أخرى وهلكت قبل أن تستقر فيها بأن وقعت في البحر ابتداء أو بالتدرج يضمن لأن الإتلاف حصل بفعله. وأشار بقوله «إلا أن يخاف الحرق» إلى أن الحريق لا بد أن يكون غالباً محيطاً بمنزل المودع ، وأما إذا لم يكن محيطاً يضمن بالدفع إلى الأجنبي؛ كذا في الخلاصة لأنه لا يخاف عليها في هذه الصورة. وفي الهداية: ولا يصدق على ذلك إلا ببينة لأنه يدعي ضرورة مسقطة للضمان بعد تحقق السبب فصار كما إذا ادعى الإذن في الإيداع ا هـ . وفي الخلاصة : إذا علم أنه وقع الحريق في بيته قبل قوله وإلا وفي الفوائد التاجية : فلو أودعها وهلكت فقال المالك هلكت عند الثاني وقال بل رده إلي وهلكت عندي لا يصدق لأن إيداع الغير موجب للضمان بخلاف ما لو غصب من المودع وهلكت فأراد المالك أن يضمن الغاصب فقال المودع قد رده إلي فهلكت عندي وقال لا بل هلكت عنده فالقول قول المودع لأنه أمين ا هـ . فلا اهـ.
Delivering it, then preventing it or mixing it with his property without permission such that it is not distinguishable: he is liable. If it mixes without his action, no. He does not sell. He does not lend. He does not lend it to others to use it for hire or sharecropping; he does not give land for sharecropping to another; the mudarib does not appoint another mudarib; the one given goods for sale (mustabdi') does not have the right to delegate; the one given goods for purchase (mustabda') does not have the right to deposit; and the tenth (case) is not mentioned. In al-Khulasa: The deposit is not to be deposited with another, nor lent, nor rented, nor pawned; if he does any of it, he is liable. The lessee may rent out, lend, and deposit. The ruling on pawning is not mentioned, and it should be that he may pawn. In al-Tajrid: The mortgagee has no right to dispose of the pawn in any way other than retention; he neither rents it out, nor wears it, nor uses it; if he does, he is a wrongdoer, but the pawn is not invalidated. His statement: (except) if he fears fire or drowning, then he may hand it over to his neighbor or another ship, because this is necessitated preservation, so he is not liable thereby. For this reason, it is stated in al-Khulasa: A woman was present at death and had a deposit, so she gave it to a neighbor of hers, and it perished with her; if at the time of her death there was no one from her family present, she is not liable. End quote. Because a method of preservation was necessitated. For this reason, they also said: If it was possible for him to preserve it at the time of fire or drowning with his family, but he gave it to a stranger, he is liable. In his statement 'and handed it over to another ship' there is an indication that if he threw it into another ship and it perished before settling in it, by falling into the sea immediately or gradually, he is liable because the destruction occurred by his action. His statement 'except if he fears fire' indicates that the fire must be prevailing and surrounding the depositary's house; but if it is not surrounding, he is liable by giving to a stranger. Thus in al-Khulasa, because he does not fear for it in this case. In al-Hidaya: He is not believed except with evidence because he claims a necessity that nullifies liability after the cause is established, so it is like when he claims permission for deposit. End quote. In al-Khulasa: If it is known that the fire occurred in his house, his statement is accepted; otherwise not. In al-Fawaid al-Tajiyya: If he deposited it (with another) and it perished, and the owner said 'It perished with the second,' and he (the depositary) said 'Rather, I returned it to him and it perished with me,' he is not believed because depositing with another necessitates liability, unlike if it was usurped from the depositary and perished, and the owner wanted to hold the usurper liable, and the depositary said 'I returned it to him and it perished with me,' and he (the usurper) said 'No, it perished with him,' then the statement of the depositary is believed because he is a trustee. End quote. So end quote.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, explaining the liability of a depositary (mudi') for unauthorized handling of the deposit. The matn states that if the depositary prevents the owner from taking the deposit, or mixes it with his own property without permission such that it becomes indistinguishable, he becomes liable (damin). However, if the mixing occurs without his action, he is not liable. The commentary then extends this to transactions: the depositary cannot sell, lend, rent, or deposit the trust with another; nor can a partner in mufawada or a sharecropper (muzari') delegate his rights. The mudarib cannot appoint another mudarib. The key principle is that the deposit is an amanah (trust), and any unauthorized disposition changing its nature as a specific trust triggers liability. The exception (ill) is if the depositary fears fire or drowning and gives the deposit to a neighbor or another ship as a preservation measure – here, no liability arises because he is acting out of necessity. Further details: if the depositary could have preserved it himself, he must do so; giving to a stranger then makes him liable. The fire must be overwhelming and surrounding. The depositary's claim of fire is accepted only with evidence because it negates liability. Examples illustrate these principles: a dying woman giving a deposit to a neighbor is absolved if no family was present; a depositary throwing goods into another ship before it settles causes liability if the goods perish mid-air. The commentary also cites al-Hidaya and al-Khulasa for evidentiary rules and a scenario where the owner claims the goods perished with a third party, showing nuanced treatment of conflicting claims.

Key Terms
وديعة (wadi‘ah): Deposit for safekeeping; a trust.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate.
امين (amin): Trustee; not liable for loss without negligence.
خلط (khalat): Mixing; if done without permission until indistinguishable, triggers liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which act does NOT make a depositary liable?
A) Lending the deposit to a friend without permission.
B) Mixing the deposit with his own property until indistinguishable.
C) Accidentally spilling water that mixes the deposit with his goods.
D) Re-depositing the deposit with another person.

Key Takeaway
The depositary becomes liable if he engages in any unauthorized disposition that alters the deposit's identity or possession.

Exception: Fear of Destruction and Evidentiary Rules
Commentary on exception for necessity and evidentiary rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the exception for necessity (fear of fire/drowning) that preserves the depositary's amanah and identify the evidentiary burden required to claim this exception.

Core Idea
Necessity (fear of total destruction) permits giving the deposit to another without liability.

The general rule is that a depositary cannot hand the deposit to a third party. However, if there is a genuine fear of fire or drowning, he may give it to a neighbor or another ship as a preservation measure. This lesson explores the conditions for this exception and the rules of evidence when the exception is claimed.

Guided Path
1
Recall the general prohibition
The depositary may not deposit the trust with another person. Doing so without necessity makes him liable.
This is the default.
2
Identify the exception: necessity
The text states 'except if he fears fire or drowning'. This is a necessity that forces him to act. He may hand it to a neighbor or another ship.
The fear must be well-founded.
3
Define the conditions for the exception
The fire must be overwhelming and surrounding his house. If it is not surrounding, he must preserve it himself and giving to a stranger makes him liable. Also, if he could have used his own family to save it, he must, otherwise gives to a stranger.
Necessity is measured objectively.
4
Analyze the evidentiary burden
If the depositary claims he gave the deposit due to fear, his word is not accepted without evidence (bayyinah) because he is claiming a reason that nullifies liability after the cause (the unauthorized act) existed. This is from al-Hidaya.
The burden is on him to prove the necessity.

When Refusal to Return a Deposit Makes the Depositary Liable
Ruling on liability for unjust refusal to return a deposit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a depositary becomes liable for refusing to return a deposit, and apply the rule to simple scenarios.

Core Idea
Unjust refusal to return a deposit when able makes the depositary liable.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on liability for refusing to return a deposit after the owner demands it. The general rule is that if the depositary is able to deliver but refuses without a valid cause, he becomes a guarantor (damin). However, the ruling hinges on the concept of oppression (zulm); if the refusal is not oppressive (e.g., to prevent sin), liability is negated. We will analyze the conditions for liability and understand the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is refusing to return a deposit after the owner's demand while able. The ruling is that the depositary becomes liable (damin) because he is oppressive (zalim).
2
Identify the condition for liability
The condition is that the refusal must be oppressive (zulm). If not oppressive, no liability.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The depositary is an amin (trustee); refusing unjustly transforms him into a usurper (damin) since he prevents the owner's right.
4
Apply the rule
Given a scenario where the owner demands, the depositary able but refuses for no valid reason, he is liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن طلبها ربها فحبسها قادراً على تسليمها فمنعها) يعني لو منع صاحب الوديعة بعد طلبه وهو قادر على تسليمها يكون ضامناً لأنه ظالم بالمنع حتى لو لم يكن ظالماً بالمنع لا يضمن ولهذا قال قاضيخان في فتاواه : لو كانت الوديعة سيفاً فأراد صاحبه أن يأخذه من المودع ليضرب به رجلاً ظلماً فإنه لا يدفعه إليه لما فيه من الإعانة على الظلم، ولو أودعت كتاباً فيه إقرار منها للزوج بمال أو بقبض مهرها من الزوج فللمودع أن لا يدفع الكتاب إليها لما فيه من ذهاب حق الزوج اهـ.
His statement: (And if its owner demands it and he detains it while able to deliver it, then he prevents it) means that if he prevents the deposit's owner after his demand while he is able to deliver it, he becomes liable because he is oppressor by prevention. So if he is not oppressor by prevention, he is not liable. For this reason, Qadikhan said in his Fatawa: If the deposit was a sword and its owner wanted to take it from the depositary to strike a wrongfully with it, then he should not give it to him because that would be aiding in oppression. And if a book containing her acknowledgement of a debt to the husband or of receipt of her dower from the husband was deposited, the depositary may refuse to give the book to her because it entails loss of the husband's right. End quote.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's ruling that if the owner demands the deposit and the depositary, being able to deliver, refuses, he becomes liable (damin). The key is that he must be able to deliver; if unable (e.g., due to distance or impossibility), no liability. The depositary's refusal must be oppressive; if there is a valid reason, liability is negated. Qadikhan provides two exceptions: (1) if the deposit is a sword and the owner wants it to commit oppression, the depositary must refuse to prevent sin; (2) if the deposit is a book containing a wife's acknowledgement of a debt to her husband, refusing to give it to her is permissible to protect the husband's right. Thus, the general rule of liability for refusal applies only when refusal is unjust and harmful to the owner.

Key Terms
ظالم (zalim): One who wrongfully prevents the owner's right to his property; oppression.
إعانة على الظلم (i‘anah ‘ala al-zulm): Aiding in oppression; forbidden even if the depositary is an amin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a depositary become liable for refusing to return a deposit?
A) Anytime he refuses, even if unable.
B) Only if able and refusal is unjust.
C) Only if the owner demands in court.
D) Only if the deposit is valuable.

Key Takeaway
A depositary is liable for refusing to return a deposit only when he is able to deliver and his refusal is unjust (oppressive).

Exceptions: Permissible Refusal to Return a Deposit
Exceptions to liability for refusing to return a deposit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two exceptions from Qadikhan's Fatawa where a depositary may refuse to return a deposit without liability, and apply the principle of 'aiding in oppression' (i'anah ala al-zulm).

Core Idea
A depositary may refuse to return a deposit if returning would aid oppression or cause harm to a third party's right.

The general rule states that unjust refusal makes the depositary liable. However, Qadikhan provides two exceptions: (1) when the deposit is a sword and the owner wants it to commit oppression, and (2) when the deposit is a book containing a wife's acknowledgement that could harm her husband's right. In these cases, refusal is not oppressive; rather, it is obligatory or permissible to prevent sin or protect rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exceptions
Two cases: sword (to prevent oppression) and book (to protect husband's right).
2
Understand the underlying principle
The depositary must not aid in sin (i'anah ala al-zulm). If returning the deposit facilitates sin, it is forbidden, and hence refusal is justified.
3
Apply the principle
In any scenario where returning the deposit would likely lead to harm or sin, the depositary may refuse.
4
Note the limitation
The refusal must be based on a genuine risk, not mere suspicion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن طلبها ربها فحبسها قادراً على تسليمها فمنعها) يعني لو منع صاحب الوديعة بعد طلبه وهو قادر على تسليمها يكون ضامناً لأنه ظالم بالمنع حتى لو لم يكن ظالماً بالمنع لا يضمن ولهذا قال قاضيخان في فتاواه : لو كانت الوديعة سيفاً فأراد صاحبه أن يأخذه من المودع ليضرب به رجلاً ظلماً فإنه لا يدفعه إليه لما فيه من الإعانة على الظلم، ولو أودعت كتاباً فيه إقرار منها للزوج بمال أو بقبض مهرها من الزوج فللمودع أن لا يدفع الكتاب إليها لما فيه من ذهاب حق الزوج اهـ.
His statement: (And if its owner demands it and he detains it while able to deliver it, then he prevents it) means that if he prevents the deposit's owner after his demand while he is able to deliver it, he becomes liable because he is oppressor by prevention. So if he is not oppressor by prevention, he is not liable. For this reason, Qadikhan said in his Fatawa: If the deposit was a sword and its owner wanted to take it from the depositary to strike a wrongfully with it, then he should not give it to him because that would be aiding in oppression. And if a book containing her acknowledgement of a debt to the husband or of receipt of her dower from the husband was deposited, the depositary may refuse to give the book to her because it entails loss of the husband's right. End quote.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's ruling that if the owner demands the deposit and the depositary, being able to deliver, refuses, he becomes liable (damin). The key is that he must be able to deliver; if unable (e.g., due to distance or impossibility), no liability. The depositary's refusal must be oppressive; if there is a valid reason, liability is negated. Qadikhan provides two exceptions: (1) if the deposit is a sword and the owner wants it to commit oppression, the depositary must refuse to prevent sin; (2) if the deposit is a book containing a wife's acknowledgement of a debt to her husband, refusing to give it to her is permissible to protect the husband's right. Thus, the general rule of liability for refusal applies only when refusal is unjust and harmful to the owner.

Key Terms
ظالم (zalim): One who wrongfully prevents the owner's right to his property; oppression.
إعانة على الظلم (i‘anah ‘ala al-zulm): Aiding in oppression; forbidden even if the depositary is an amin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Qadikhan, when may a depositary refuse to return a deposit?
A) Only if the deposit is a weapon.
B) Only if the owner is a known criminal.
C) If returning would aid oppression or harm a third party's right.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
A depositary may, and in some cases must, refuse to return a deposit if doing so would aid oppression or unjustly harm another's right.
