When a Trustee's Death Without Disclosure Turns a Trust into a Liability
Expansion of the rule that tajhil turns amanat into liability, with exceptions and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi rule that a trustee's death without informing the heirs (tajhil) makes the trust liable, and identify the four exceptions recognized in Hanafi fatwa works.

Core Idea
A trustee's silence at death creates liability unless the trustee is a mosque supervisor, sultan, qadi, or mufawada partner.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on a key principle: if a trustee dies without informing his heirs about a deposit in his care, the trust becomes a liability on his estate. However, not all trustees are treated equally. The text, drawing from Qadikhan and others, lists specific roles that are exempt, and also conditions when liability does not arise. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correctly applying the law of trusts (amanat) in estate matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage begins with Qadikhan's statement: trusts become liable (mamunah) due to tajhil. Note that tajhil means the trustee dies without informing the heirs about the deposit, leaving them ignorant.
This is the default rule: tajhil = liability.
2
List the exceptions from Qadikhan
Qadikhan mentions three exceptions: (1) the mosque supervisor (mutawalli al-masjid) who takes from mosque revenues, (2) the sultan distributing spoils of war, and (3) the qadi handling orphan property. These are exempt from liability even if they die without clarifying.
These roles are considered public trustees, not personal trustees.
3
Add the fourth exception from Muhammad al-Shaybani
Al-Walwaliji's Fatawa replaces the qadi exception with another: a partner in mufawada (unlimited partnership). If one partner dies without clarifying, he does not guarantee the surviving partner's share. Combining Qadikhan and Walwaliji gives four exceptions.
The qadi exception is still accepted by some; the four are reconciled.
4
Understand the conditions that remove liability
The passage adds conditions: (a) if the heir already knows about the deposit, the trustee's silence does not cause liability; (b) if the trustee was genuinely unable to deliver (e.g., the item was not present), he is not liable; (c) if the owner leaves satisfied after being told 'I cannot bring it now,' it becomes a new deposit with no liability.
Liability only arises when the trustee had control and knowledge but omitted clarification.
5
Distinguish between the depositary and others
The text ends with a note that if an agent demands the deposit, the depositary is not liable to the agent because the agent is not the owner. Also, if the depositary guides a thief to the deposit, he is liable, but the heir is not.
Liability is owed to the owner, not to intermediaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المنع ظلماً موته مجهلاً ولهذا قال قاضيخان : الأمانات تنقلب مضمونة عن تجهيل إلا في ثلاث أحدها متولي المسجد إذا أخذ من غلات المسجد ومات من غير بيان لا يكون ضامناً والثانية السلطان إذا خرج إلى الغزو وغنموا وأودع بعض الغنيمة عند بعض الغانمين ومات ولم يبين عند من أودع لا ضمان عليه. والثالثة القاضي إذا أخذ مال اليتيم وأودع غيره ثم مات ولم يبين عند من أودع لا ضمان عليه، ولو أن قاضياً قبل مال اليتيم ووضعه في بيته ثم مات القاضي ولم يبين ذكر هشام عن يضمن ا هـ . وذكر الولوالجي في فتاواه أن الأمانات تنقلب مضمونة بالتجهيل إلا في ثلاثة ولم يذكر مسألة القاضي وذكر بدلها ما ذكره محمد في كتاب الشركة أحد المتفاوضين إذا مات ولم يبين حال المال الذي في يده لم يضمن نصيب شريكه ا هـ . فتحصل أن المسائل المستثناة أربعة. وقيد في الخلاصة ضمان المودع بموته مجهلاً بأن لا يعرفها الوارث، أما إذا عرفها والمودع يعلم أنه يعرف فمات ولم يبين لم يضمن. ولو قال الوارث أنا علمتها وأنكر الطالب إن فسر الوديعة وقال الوديعة كذا وأنا علمتها وقد هلكت صدق هذا، وما لو كانت الدراهم عنده فقال هلكت سواء إلا في خصلة وهي أن الوارث إذا دل السارق على الوديعة لا يضمن والمودع إذا دل ضمن وقيد بقوله قادراً على تسليمها لأنه لو منعها للعجز عن التسليم لا يضمن فلو طلبها منه فقال لا يمكنني أن أحضرها الساعة فتركها وذهب إن ترك عن رضا وذهب لا يضمن لأنه لما ذهب فقد أنشأ الوديعة، وإن كان عن غير رضا يضمن ؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وينبغي أن يكون محل هذا التفصيل ما إذا كان المودع يمكنه وكان كاذباً في قوله، أما إذا كان صادقاً فلا يضمن مطلقاً لما قلنا ولو كان الذي طلبها وكيلاً يضمن لأنه ليس له
And from oppressive prevention is his death without informing (tajhil). For this reason, Qadikhan said: Trusts become liable (mamunah) due to tajhil except in three: one is the supervisor of the mosque if he takes from the produce of the mosque and dies without clarification, he is not liable; the second is the sultan if he goes out to war and they obtain spoils and he deposits some of the spoils with some of the warriors and dies without clarifying with whom he deposited, no liability; the third is the qadi if he takes the orphan's property and deposits it with another and then dies without clarifying with whom he deposited, no liability. But if a qadi accepted the orphan's property and placed it in his house and then the qadi died without clarifying, Hisham narrated that he is liable. End quote. Al-Walwaliji mentioned in his Fatawa that amanat become liable with tajhil except in three, and he did not mention the case of the qadi but replaced it with what Muhammad mentioned in the book of partnership: one of the partners in mufawada if he dies without clarifying the state of the property in his hand, he does not guarantee the share of his partner. End quote. So it is established that the excepted cases are four. And it is conditioned in al-Khulasa: the depositary's liability due to his death without informing is when the heir does not know it; but if he (the heir) knows it and the depositary knows that he knows, then he dies without clarifying, he is not liable. And if the heir says 'I know it' and the claimant denies, if he specifies the deposit and says 'the deposit is such and such, and I know it, and it has perished,' he is believed. And likewise if the deposited dirhams were with him and he said they perished, it is the same except in one matter: that if the heir guides the thief to the deposit, he is not liable, whereas the depositary if he guides (the thief) he is liable. And he conditioned (the liability) by his ability to deliver, because if he prevents due to inability to deliver, he is not liable. So if he demands it from him and he says 'I cannot bring it right now,' and he leaves it and goes away, if he left with satisfaction and went, he is not liable because when he went, he initiated a new deposit; but if it was without satisfaction, he is liable. Thus in al-Khulasa. And it should be that the place of this detail is when the depositary is able and lying in his statement; but if he is truthful, he is not liable at all as we said. And if it was an agent who demanded it, he is liable because he does not have...
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific type of oppression: the depositary's death without informing the heirs about the deposit (tajhil). Generally, if a trustee (amin) dies without explaining, his heirs are left ignorant, and the trust becomes a liability (mamdunah) on his estate. However, Qadikhan and others list exceptions: (1) the mosque supervisor (mutawalli) who takes from mosque revenues – he is a trustee for the mosque, not for specific individuals; (2) the sultan who distributes spoils – his deposit is part of public trust; (3) the qadi who handles orphan property – he acts as a guardian; (4) a partner in mufawada (mutual partnership) as mentioned by Muhammad al-Shaybani – the surviving partner may have knowledge. The text also clarifies conditions: if the heir already knows the deposit, the depositary's silence does not cause liability. The heir's claim about the deposit's nature is accepted if specified. Additionally, if the depositary is unable to deliver due to genuine inability (e.g., not having it immediately), no liability arises. A special case: if the owner leaves satisfied after being told 'I cannot bring it now,' it becomes a new deposit, so no liability; if unsatisfied, the depositary becomes liable. The passage ends with a discussion about an agent demanding the deposit – likely the depositary is not liable to the agent because the agent is not the owner, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
تجهيل (tajhil): Death without informing the heirs about the deposit, leaving them ignorant.
أمانة (amanah): A trust; property held without liability for loss unless negligence occurs.
متفاوضان (mutafawidan): Partners in mufawada (unlimited partnership); each is a trustee for the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT an exception to the rule that a trustee becomes liable for dying without informing about the trust?
A) The mosque supervisor (mutawalli al-masjid).
B) The sultan when distributing spoils.
C) The guardian of an orphan's property (qadi).
D) A partner in a company (mudarib).

Key Takeaway
A trustee's death without disclosure (tajhil) triggers liability unless the trustee falls under one of four exceptions: mosque supervisor, sultan, qadi, or mufawada partner.

Depositary's Refusal to Return Deposit to Prevent Injustice
Two rulings on withholding a deposit to prevent injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios in which a depositary must or may refuse to return a deposit, and explain the legal principle of preventing assistance to injustice.

Core Idea
A depositary must withhold a deposit if returning it would assist oppression or destroy a third party's right.

This lesson examines two rulings from Imam Muhammad regarding the depositary's duty. The general rule is that a depositary must return the deposit on demand. However, when returning would enable the depositor to commit injustice or cause loss of another's right, the depositary is justified—and even obliged—to withhold it. These exceptions are grounded in the higher obligation to prevent harm and not aid wrongdoing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first scenario
The deposit is a weapon intended for unjust aggression. The text rules: 'he does not give it to him because that would be assistance in injustice.' Determine the legal consequence: giving is prohibited, withholding is obligatory.
Source: 'من المودع ليضرب به رجلاً ظلماً'
2
Identify the second scenario
The deposit is a book containing a wife's acknowledgment of debt or receipt of mahr. The text says: 'the depositary may not hand over the book to her because it would entail loss of the husband's right.' Note that the ruling here is expressed as a permission, but the underlying effect is the same: delivering is harmful, so withholding is the correct action.
Source: 'كتاباً فيه إقرار منها للزوج'
3
Extract the common principle
Both scenarios are based on the maxim 'No assistance in injustice' (لا إعانة على الظلم). The depositary must not become an instrument of harm, even if it means temporarily violating the trust obligation.
This principle overrides the general duty to return the deposit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محمد أنه من المودع ليضرب به رجلاً ظلماً فإنه لا يدفعه إليه لما فيه من الإعانة على الظلم، ولو أودعت كتاباً فيه إقرار منها للزوج بمال أو بقبض مهرها من الزوج فللمودع أن لا يدفع الكتاب إليها لما فيه من ذهاب حق الزوج اهـ.
Muhammad: If a depositor deposits [a weapon] with the depositary to strike a man unjustly, then he does not give it to him because that would be assistance in injustice. And if a book is deposited containing an acknowledgment from her [the depositor] for the husband of a debt or of receipt of her mahr from the husband, then the depositary may not hand over the book to her because it would entail loss of the husband's right.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage from Al-Ḥidāyah presents two rulings about the depositary's right to refuse returning the deposit to prevent harm. First: if someone deposits a weapon to be used for unjust aggression, the depositary must not hand it over, as that would be cooperating in sin. Second: if a wife deposits a book containing an acknowledgment that she owes her husband money or that she received her mahr (which the husband might use as evidence), the depositary may withhold it from her to protect the husband's right. These rulings are based on the principle that one should not assist in injustice, even if it means temporarily withholding a trust.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadīʿah): A trust deposit: property given to another for safekeeping without compensation.
منع (manʿ): Refusal to deliver the deposit to the depositor or owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whenever the depositary suspects the depositor will misuse it.
B) Only if returning would assist in oppression or destroy another's right.
C) Whenever the depositary is busy and cannot retrieve it.
D) Only if the depositor is not the rightful owner.

Key Takeaway
A depositary must not return a deposit if it would assist oppression or destroy a third party's right; the obligation to prevent harm overrides the duty to return.

The Rule of Tajhil and Its Exceptions in Hanafi Fiqh
Statement of the rule with exceptions from Qadikhan and Walwalji

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that amanah becomes guaranteed upon tajhil and list the four exceptions where liability is not imposed.

Core Idea
Trusts become guaranteed upon death without clarification, except for public officials acting in special capacity.

This passage addresses a critical rule regarding trust property: if a person dies without clarifying the details of property entrusted to them (tajhil), they become liable for it. However, the rule is not absolute. The Hanafi school recognizes exceptions for certain officials, such as mosque mutawallis, sultans, qadis, and partners in a mufawadah partnership. Understanding these exceptions is essential for correctly applying liability in cases of unaccounted trust property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The rule is that trusts become guaranteed (maḍmūnah) by tajhīl — the trustee dying without clarifying the trust property. The condition triggering liability is the lack of clarification, which causes negligence and prevents the rightful owner from claiming.
Tajhīl refers to dying without specifying the location, holder, or details of the trust.
2
List Qadikhan's three exceptions
Qadikhan cites three cases where no liability arises: (1) the mutawalli of a mosque who takes from its revenues and dies without clarification; (2) the sultan who goes to war and deposits booty with ghazis and dies without specifying; (3) the qadi who takes an orphan's property and deposits it with someone else and dies without clarification.
These exceptions are based on the public nature of their roles and the difficulty of accounting.
3
Note al-Walwalji's alternative exceptions
Al-Walwalji in his Fatawa also mentions three exceptions but replaces the qadi case with a partner in a mufawadah partnership who dies without clarifying the partnership property. He does not mention the qadi case.
This shows a difference in the exceptions listed by two authoritative Hanafi jurists.
4
Consolidate the four exceptions
By combining Qadikhan's three and al-Walwalji's replacement, the total exceptions become four: mutawalli, sultan, qadi, and mufawadah partner. The passage concludes 'فتحصل أن المسائل المستثناة أربعة' (Thus the excepted cases total four).
The qadi exception remains from Qadikhan; Walwalji added the partner case.
5
Understand the reasoning
The exceptions exist because these officials act in a general capacity and are entrusted with overseeing communal or public funds; their death without specification is excused due to the inherent difficulty of maintaining perfect records, unlike ordinary trustees who have a more direct duty to clarify.
The underlying 'illa' (effective cause) is the public benefit and the overwhelming nature of their duties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المنع ظلماً موته مجهلاً ولهذا قال قاضيخان : الأمانات تنقلب مضمونة عن تجهيل إلا في ثلاث أحدها متولي المسجد إذا أخذ من غلات المسجد ومات من غير بيان لا يكون ضامناً والثانية السلطان إذا خرج إلى الغزو وغنموا وأودع بعض الغنيمة عند بعض الغانمين ومات ولم يبين عند من أودع لا ضمان عليه. والثالثة القاضي إذا أخذ مال اليتيم وأودع غيره ثم مات ولم يبين عند من أودع لا ضمان عليه. ولو أن قاضياً قبل مال اليتيم ووضعه في بيته ثم مات القاضي ولم يبين ذكر هشام عن يضمن ا هـ . وذكر الولوالجي في فتاواه أن الأمانات تنقلب مضمونة بالتجهيل إلا في ثلاثة ولم يذكر مسألة القاضي وذكر بدلها ما ذكره محمد في كتاب الشركة أحد المتفاوضين إذا مات ولم يبين حال المال الذي في يده لم يضمن نصيب شريكه ا هـ . فتحصل أن المسائل المستثناة أربعة.
And from the prohibition of oppression, his dying without clarification. For this reason Qadikhan said: Trusts become guaranteed due to tajhil (making unknown) except in three [cases]: one, the mutawalli of a mosque who takes from the mosque's revenues and dies without clarification is not liable; second, the sultan who goes to war and they take booty and some of the booty is deposited with some of the ghazis and he dies without clarifying with whom he deposited — no liability; third, the qadi who takes an orphan's property and deposits it with someone else then dies without clarifying with whom he deposited — no liability. And if a qadi receives an orphan's property and places it in his house then dies without clarifying, Hisham mentioned that he is liable. And al-Walwalji mentioned in his Fatawa that trusts become guaranteed by tajhil except in three, and he did not mention the qadi case; instead he mentioned what Muhammad mentioned in Kitab al-Shirkah: one of the two partners in a mutual partnership (mufawadah) if he dies without clarifying the state of the property in his hand, he does not guarantee his partner's share. So it is established that the excepted cases are four.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage from Al-Ḥidāyah (or its commentary) discusses the rule that a trustee (e.g., depositary, guardian) who dies without specifying the details of the trust (tajhil) becomes liable (guarantor) for the property, because his negligence causes loss. However, there are exceptions where even death without clarification does not impose liability. Qadikhan lists three: (1) the mosque administrator (mutawalli) who manages mosque revenues; (2) the sultan who distributes booty and dies without recording deposits; (3) the qadi who takes orphan's property and deposits it elsewhere. Al-Walwalji substitutes the qadi case with a fourth: a partner in a mufawadah partnership who dies without clarifying the partnership property does not guarantee his partner's share. Thus the total exceptions reach four. The underlying reasoning is that these officials act in a general capacity and their death without specification is excused due to the difficulty of accounting, unlike ordinary individuals.

Key Terms
تجهيل (tajhīl): Dying without clarifying the details of the trust property, such as its location or with whom it was deposited.
مضمونة (maḍmūnah): Guaranteed: the trustee becomes liable to compensate for the loss if the property is not returned due to his own negligence or actions.
متولي المسجد (mutawallī al-masjid): The administrator or trustee of a mosque who manages its revenues and affairs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many exceptions to the rule of tajhil are ultimately established?
A) Three
B) Four
C) One
D) Two

Key Takeaway
Trusts become guaranteed upon tajhil except for four specific public officials: mutawalli, sultan, qadi, and mufawadah partner.

Qualifications of the Depositary's Guarantee upon Death
Qualification of depositary's guarantee upon death with two conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two qualifications to the depositary's guarantee upon death (heir's knowledge and inability to deliver) and apply them to scenarios involving heir knowledge and owner's consent.

Core Idea
The depositary's guarantee upon death is lifted if the heir knows the deposit or the depositary is unable to deliver and the owner leaves willingly.

The general rule states that a depositary who dies without clarifying the deposit becomes liable. However, this rule is qualified in two ways. First, if the heir already knows the deposit, the depositary is not liable because the property is known. Second, if the depositary is genuinely unable to deliver the deposit at the time of demand, and the owner leaves it willingly, the depositary is not liable because a new deposit is formed. This passage from al-Khulasah presents these exceptions, along with a distinction between the heir and the depositary in case of directing a thief.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two qualifications
Find the two scenarios where the depositary is not liable upon death: (1) when the heir knows the deposit, (2) when the depositary is unable to deliver and the owner leaves willingly. Notice the words 'مات ولم يبين لم يضمن' and 'عجز عن التسليم لا يضمن'.
The first qualification relates to the heir's knowledge; the second to the depositary's ability.
2
Understand the reasoning for the heir's knowledge
The text says: if the heir knows the deposit and the depositary knows that he knows, then death without clarification incurs no liability. Why? Because the property is already known to the heir, so there is no loss of property due to the depositary's death.
If the heir describes the deposit and claims it perished, he is believed.
3
Analyze the inability to deliver scenario
If the depositary says 'I cannot bring it now' and the owner leaves it and goes away willingly, it is a new deposit. The depositary is not liable for the original deposit because the owner voluntarily re-deposited. But if the owner leaves unwillingly, the depositary remains liable.
Distinguish between willing and unwilling departure.
4
Examine the thief-diliction case
The text notes an exception: if the heir directs a thief to the deposit, he is not liable, but the depositary is liable. The heir is not a trustee, so his act is merely a sin; the depositary violates trust.
This highlights the difference in liability between a trustee and a non-trustee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد في الخلاصة ضمان المودع بموته مجهلاً بأن لا يعرفها الوارث، أما إذا عرفها والمودع يعلم أنه يعرف فمات ولم يبين لم يضمن. ولو قال الوارث أنا علمتها وأنكر الطالب إن فسر الوديعة وقال الوديعة كذا وأنا علمتها وقد هلكت صدق هذا، وما لو كانت الدراهم عنده فقال هلكت سواء إلا في خصلة وهي أن الوارث إذا دل السارق على الوديعة لا يضمن والمودع إذا دل ضمن وقيد بقوله قادراً على تسليمها لأنه لو منعها للعجز عن التسليم لا يضمن فلو طلبها منه فقال لا يمكنني أن أحضرها الساعة فتركها وذهب إن ترك عن رضا وذهب لا يضمن لأنه لما ذهب فقد أنشأ الوديعة، وإن كان عن غير رضا يضمن؛ كذا في الخلاصة.
And al-Khulasah restricted the depositary's guarantee upon his dying while unknown to the case where the heir does not know the deposit. If the heir knows it and the depositary knows that he knows, then he dies without clarifying, he is not liable. And if the heir says 'I knew it' and the claimant denies — if he describes the deposit and says 'the deposit is such and such and I knew it, and it perished,' he is believed. [This applies] similarly to when dirhams are with him and he says they perished, except for one case: the heir if he directs the thief to the deposit is not liable, whereas the depositary if he directs [the thief] is liable. And he restricted it [the guarantee] to his being able to deliver it, because if he withholds [the deposit] due to inability to deliver, he is not liable. So if [the owner] demands it from him and he says 'I cannot bring it now,' and he leaves it and goes away — if he left it willingly and went, he is not liable, because when he went he has initiated a new deposit; but if it was unwillingly, he is liable. Thus in al-Khulasah.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage from al-Khulasah (a Hanafi fiqh work) clarifies the rule of guarantee upon death with tajhil. The earlier rule that a depositary who dies without clarifying becomes liable is qualified: if the heir already knows the details of the deposit, then no liability attaches because the property is known. Additionally, the depositary's liability is further restricted to cases where he is able to deliver the deposit. If he is genuinely unable (e.g., the deposit is far away), and the owner leaves it willingly after being told, that constitutes a new deposit (the owner re-deposits with the depositary), so no liability for the previous deposit. But if the owner leaves unwillingly (e.g., forced by circumstances), the depositary remains liable for failing to deliver. The passage also distinguishes between the heir and the depositary in case of directing a thief to the deposit: the heir is not liable because he is not a trustee, but the depositary is liable because he violates his trust.

Key Terms
الوراث (al-wārith): The heir of the deceased depositary who knows the deposit's details.
عجز عن التسليم (ʿajz ʿan al-taslīm): Inability to physically deliver the deposit at the time of demand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a depositary become liable if he says 'I cannot bring it now' and the owner leaves willingly?
A) He becomes liable immediately.
B) He is not liable because the owner re-deposits it.
C) He is liable if the deposit is later lost.
D) He is liable only if the owner described the deposit.

Key Takeaway
The depositary's guarantee upon death is lifted if the heir knows the deposit or if the depositary is unable to deliver and the owner leaves willingly.

Depositary's Liability When Proxy Demands Deposit
Intra-Hanafi dispute on depositary liability when proxy demands deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the basis of the intra-Hanafi dispute on depositary liability when a proxy demands the deposit and apply the commentator's reconciliation method.

Core Idea
A depositary's liability for refusing a proxy turns on whether the refusal continues the original deposit or initiates a new one.

When an owner sends a proxy to retrieve a deposit, the depositary may hesitate. Does refusal lead to liability if the deposit is lost? This lesson examines two conflicting Hanafi rulings and the method of reconciliation used to resolve them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting rulings
Al-Khulasah holds the depositary liable for refusing the proxy; Qadi Dhahir and al-Tajnis hold he is not liable if the refusal aims to deliver only to the owner.
Both are within the Hanafi school.
2
Analyse the reasoning behind each ruling
Al-Khulasah: The proxy cannot initiate a new deposit, so any willing departure creates an invalid new deposit, making the depositary liable. In contrast, the other view sees the refusal as preserving the original deposit, so no liability.
The key is whether a new deposit is created.
3
Understand the proxy vs messenger distinction
A messenger (rasul) is like the owner's own speech; withholding from a messenger is like withholding from the owner. A proxy (wakil) has independent authority but cannot create a new deposit.
This distinction may affect the ruling.
4
Apply the commentator's reconciliation
If the proxy agrees to later delivery (intending a new deposit), al-Khulasah's ruling applies. If the depositary insists on delivering to the owner only (no new deposit), the other ruling applies.
Thus the rulings are not contradictory but contextual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان الذي طلبها وكيلاً يضمن لأنه ليس له أن ينشي الوديعة بخلاف المالك. قال الرملي قال في الخلاصة: المالك إذا طلب الوديعة فقال المودع لا يمكنني أن أحضرها الساعة فتركها وذهب؛ إن تركها عن رضا فهلكت لا يضمن لأنه لما ذهب فقد أنشأ الوديعة، وإن كان عن غير رضا يضمن، ولو كان الذي طلب الوديعة وكيل المالك يضمن لأنه ليس له إنشاء الوديعة بخلاف المالك اهـ. وهذا صريح في أنه يضمن بعدم الدفع إلى وكيل المالك كما لا يخفى. وفي العمادية معزياً إلى الظهيرية: ورسول المودع إذا طلب الوديعة فقال لا أدفع إلا للذي جاء بها ولم يدفع إلى الرسول حتى هلكت ضمن. وذكر في فتاوى قاضي ظهير هذه المسألة، وأجاب نجم الدين أنه يضمن وفيه نظر بدليل أن المودع إذا صدق من ادعى أنه وكيل بقبض الوديعة فإنه قال في الوكالة: لا يؤمر بدفع الوديعة إليه ولكن لقائل أن يفرق بين الوكيل والرسول لأن الرسول ينطق على لسان المرسل ولا كذلك الوكيل ألا ترى أنه لو عزل الوكيل قبل علم الوكيل بالعزل لا يصح، رجع عن الرسالة قبل علم الرسول بالرجوع صح؛ كذا في فتاواه اهـ. أقول: ظاهر ما نقله في الفصول العمادية معزياً إلى قاضي ظهير أنه لا يضمن في مسألة الوكيل كما هو منقول عن التجنيس فهو مخالف لما في الخلاصة كما هو ظاهره، ويتراءى لي التوفيق بين القولين بأن يحمل ما في الخلاصة على ما إذا قصد الوكيل إنشاء الوديعة عند المودع بعد منعه ليدفع له في وقت آخر، وما في فتاوى قاضي ظهير والتجنيس على ما إذا منع ليؤدي إلى المودع بنفسه ولذلك قال في جوابه: إلا للذي جاء بها. وفي الخلاصة ما هو صريح في أن الوكيل تركها وذهب عن رضا بعد قول المودع لا يمكنني أن أحضرها الساعة أي وأدفعها لك في غير هذه الساعة فإذا فارقه فقد أنشأ الإيداع وليس له ذلك بخلاف قوله لا أدفعها إلا للذي جاء بها فإنه استبقاء للإيداع الأول لا إنشاء إيداع فتأمل.
And if the one who requested it is a proxy, he [the depositary] is liable because he [the proxy] does not have the right to initiate a new deposit, unlike the owner. Al-Ramli said: al-Khulasah said: If the owner demands the deposit and the depositary says 'I cannot bring it now' and he leaves it and goes away — if he left it willingly, it perishes, he is not liable because when he went he has initiated a deposit; and if it was unwillingly, he is liable. And if the one who demanded the deposit is the owner's proxy, he is liable because he does not have the right to initiate a new deposit, unlike the owner. This is explicit that he is liable for not delivering to the owner's proxy. And in al-'Imadiyyah quoting al-Dhahiriyyah: The messenger of the depositary if he requests the deposit and he says 'I will not give except to the one who brought it' and he does not give to the messenger until it perishes, he is liable. And it is mentioned in the Fatawa of Qadi Dhahir this issue, and Najm al-Din answered that he is liable, and there is consideration in it, on the evidence that if the depositary believes someone who claims to be a proxy for receiving the deposit, he said in the chapter of agency: he is not ordered to give the deposit to him. But one could distinguish between the proxy and the messenger, because the messenger speaks on the tongue of the sender, and not so the proxy. Do you not see that if the proxy is dismissed before his knowledge of the dismissal, it is not valid; if the messenger is revoked before his knowledge of the revocation, it is valid. Thus in his Fatawa. I say: The apparent meaning of what is transmitted in al-Fusul al-'Imadiyyah quoting Qadi Dhahir is that he is not liable in the case of the proxy, as is transmitted from al-Tajnis, which contradicts what is in al-Khulasah. And it appears to me a reconciliation between the two statements: to interpret what is in al-Khulasah as the case where the proxy intended to initiate a new deposit with the depositary after his refusal to deliver at a later time; and what is in the Fatawa of Qadi Dhahir and al-Tajnis as the case where he refused in order to deliver to the owner himself. Therefore he said in his answer: 'except to the one who brought it.' And in al-Khulasah it is explicit that the proxy left it and went willingly after the depositary said 'I cannot bring it now' meaning 'I will give it to you at another time,' so when he left him, he has initiated a deposit, and he does not have that right, unlike his saying 'I will not give it except to the one who brought it,' for it is a continuation of the first deposit, not an initiation of a new deposit.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the depositary's liability when a proxy (wakil) of the owner demands the deposit. According to al-Khulasah, the depositary is liable if he refuses to hand over to the proxy because the proxy cannot create a new deposit (i.e., the owner's consent to leave it is not assumed for the proxy). However, other sources (Fatawa of Qadi Dhahir, al-Tajnis) say the depositary is not liable if he withholds to give directly to the owner, as the proxy is not trusted to receive. The commentator proposes a reconciliation: al-Khulasah's case is when the proxy agrees to leave it for later (thus initiating a new deposit), while the other ruling applies when the depositary insists on delivering only to the owner (no new deposit). This resolves the apparent contradiction. The distinction between a proxy (wakil) and a mere messenger (rasul) is also noted: the messenger is a direct mouthpiece of the principal, so refusal to give to him is more serious.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): A proxy appointed by the owner to collect the deposit on his behalf.
رسول (rasūl): A messenger sent by the owner to collect the deposit; considered as the owner's own speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciliation presented, when is the depositary not liable for refusing to give the deposit to a proxy?
A) When the proxy has permission to accept delivery.
B) When the depositary refuses in order to deliver directly to the owner.
C) When the proxy leaves the deposit willingly.
D) When the proxy is a messenger rather than a wakil.

Key Takeaway
The depositary's liability when refusing a proxy hinges on whether the refusal leads to an unauthorized new deposit or continues the original custodianship.

Liability and Ownership When Depositary Mixes Deposit Without Permission
Ruling on liability when deposit mixed indistinguishably

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mixing a deposit without permission, identify the conditions triggering liability, and apply the rule to distinguish when the depositary becomes liable.

Core Idea
Mixing a deposit without permission until indistinguishable triggers liability and ownership transfer to the depositary.

When a depositary (mustawda') mixes the deposit (wadi'ah) with his own property without the owner's permission such that the mixture becomes inseparable, Hanafi law considers this a form of consumption (istihlak) that transfers ownership to the depositary, but imposes a strict obligation to compensate the owner. This lesson unpacks the ruling of Abu Hanifah as presented in Kanz al-Daqa'iq, covering the scope of mixing and the crucial exception when a third party performs the mixing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its consequence
Locate the central ruling: mixing without permission until indistinguishable makes the depositary liable (damin) and owner of the mixture.
The act is khalṭ (mixing); the result is dhaman (liability) and milk (ownership).
2
Examine the conditions for liability
Two key conditions: (1) mixing without the owner's permission, (2) making separation impossible (la tamiyyaz). Both must be present to trigger istihlak.
If the mixture could still be separated, the deposit remains a trust (amanah) and no liability.
3
Understand the legal consequences
Once liable, the depositary owns the mixture but cannot benefit until he pays the guarantee. The owner's right shifts from the specific property to a debt claim for its value.
If the owner releases (ibra'), both the right to the property and the debt are extinguished.
4
Note the scope of the ruling
The ruling applies whether the mixed items are of the same genus (jins) or different, and whether solid or liquid (e.g., oil with sesame oil, wheat with barley, silver with silver after melting).
The author (al-Musannif) made the ruling absolute (muṭlaq), covering all types of mixing.
5
Identify the exception for third-party mixing
If a stranger or someone in the depositary's household (except the depositary himself) mixes the deposit, the depositary is not liable; the third party bears liability.
This restricts liability to the actual perpetrator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو خلطها بماله بغير الإذن حتى لا تتمير ضمنها لأنه صار مستهلكاً لها وإذا ضمنها ملكها ولا تباح له قبل أداء الضمان ولا سبيل للمالك عليها عند أبي حنيفة، ولو أبرأه سقط حقه من العين والدين . أطلق المصنف فشمل خلط الجنس بجنسه أو بغير جنسه كخلط الزيت بالشيرج، والحنطة بالشعير وبالحنطة والفضة بالفضة بعد الإذابة . قيد بكون المودع الخالط لأن الخالط لو كان أجنبياً أو من في عياله لا يضمن المودع والضمان على الخالط
And if he mixes it with his own property without permission so that it cannot be distinguished, he becomes liable for it because he has consumed it. And when he becomes liable, he owns it, and it is not permissible for him [to benefit from it] before paying the guarantee, and the owner has no recourse to the specific property according to Abu Hanifah. And if he [the owner] releases him [from liability], his right to the specific property and the debt falls. The author (al-Musannif) made it absolute, so it includes mixing like with like or with unlike, such as mixing oil with sesame oil, wheat with barley or with wheat, and silver with silver after melting. It is restricted to the depositary being the mixer, because if the mixer is a stranger or someone in his household, the depositary is not liable; the liability is on the mixer.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq (or its sharh) explains the ruling when the depositary mixes the deposit with his own property in a way that makes them indistinguishable. According to Abu Hanifah, the depositary becomes liable (guarantor) because he has effectively consumed (istihlak) the deposit by making it inseparable. Once liable, he becomes the owner of the mixed property, but he cannot enjoy it until he pays the value to the owner. The owner loses his right to the specific mixed property but has a claim for its value as a debt. If the owner subsequently releases the depositary from liability, both the right to the specific property and the debt are extinguished. The ruling applies whether the mixing is of the same type (e.g., oil with oil) or different types (e.g., wheat with barley). However, the ruling applies only if the depositary himself performed the mixing; if a third party mixed it, the depositary is not liable—the third party is. This is in line with Hanafi principles: a trustee who deliberately confuses the trust property beyond recognition is considered a muta'addi (transgressor) and becomes liable.

Key Terms
خلط (khalṭ): Mixing the deposit with the depositary's own property such that the individual items become indistinguishable.
استهلاك (istihlāk): Consumption in a legal sense: making the deposit unidentifiable and thus subject to guarantee and ownership transfer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifah, what happens to the ownership of a deposit when the depositary mixes it with his own property without permission so it cannot be distinguished?
A) The depositary becomes liable and owns the mixture immediately.
B) The owner retains ownership of the mixture proportionally.
C) The deposit becomes void and both lose rights.
D) The depositary is not liable because mixing is permissible.

Key Takeaway
When a depositary mixes a deposit with his own property without permission beyond recognition, he becomes liable and owner of the mixture, and must compensate the owner before benefiting.

Depositary's Liability When Refusing Delivery
Rulings on depositary's liability for loss when he refuses to return deposit or delays delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a Hanafi depositary becomes liable for loss when he refuses to return the deposit, and apply these conditions to novel scenarios.

Core Idea
A depositary is not liable for every refusal; liability depends on the timing of loss and whether the refusal constitutes transgression.

Many assume that any delay or refusal by a depositary immediately makes him a guarantor. The Hanafi school, however, draws careful distinctions. This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq teaches us that a depositary's liability depends on specific circumstances: whether the loss occurs before or after an acknowledgment, whether the refusal to hand over to a messenger is justified, and whether the depositary has a duty to transport the deposit. By the end of this lesson, you will navigate these nuances with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The passage presents four distinct scenarios involving the depositary's refusal. List them: (1) asking to wait until tomorrow, (2) asking to carry it today, (3) refusing the owner's messenger without a guarantor, (4) preventing the master from taking the slave's deposit.
2
Extract the ruling for each
For each scenario, determine whether the depositary is liable (daman) or not. Note the precise wording: 'لا ضمان' (no liability) vs 'ضمن' (liable).
3
Identify the rationale
The commentary provides reasons: in scenario 1, liability depends on timing relative to acknowledgment; in scenario 2, the owner bears transport cost; in scenario 3, the messenger may not be trustworthy; in scenario 4, the master may not own the deposit.
4
Note the authority level
In scenario 3, the text says 'على ظاهر الرواية' (according to the apparent narration). This indicates an intra-Hanafi difference. Understand that the prevailing (mufta bihi) view is that the depositary is not liable.
5
Synthesize the principle
From these rulings, derive a general principle: the depositary must not act oppressively (zulm) or negligently (taqsir). His duty is to keep the deposit safely and return it upon demand, but he is not an insurer. Certain refusals are justified and do not trigger liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنشاء الوديعة بخلاف المالك، ولو قال له بعد طلبه اطلبها غداً ثم ادعى ضياعها، فإن قال ضاعت بعد الإقرار لا ضمان وإلا ضمن ،ولو قال له احملها إلى اليوم فمضى ولم يحملها لا لأن مؤنة الرد على المالك، ولو منعها من رسول المالك وقال لا أدفعها إلا إلى الذي يضمن جاء بها لا يضمن على ظاهر الرواية كمنعه بعد قوله من جاءك وبين علامة كذا فادفعها إليه فبين رجل تلك العلامة ولم يدفع إليه حتى هلكت لا يضمن ومنعه منه وديعة عبده لا يكون ظلماً لأن المولى ليس له قبض وديعة عبده مأذوناً كان أو محجوراً ما لم يحضر ويظهر أنه من كسبه لاحتمال أنه مال الغير وديعة فإذا ظهر أنه للعبد بالبينة فحينئذ يأخذ؛ كذا في الخلاصة.
Establishing a deposit (by the depositary) is different from the owner. And if he [the owner] said to him after his request [for the deposit], 'Ask for it tomorrow,' then he [the depositary] claimed its loss, then if he said 'It was lost after the acknowledgment' there is no liability, otherwise he is liable. And if he said to him, 'Carry it to today,' and the day passed and he did not carry it, he is not liable because the expense of return is on the owner. And if he prevented it from the owner's messenger and said, 'I will not hand it over except to the one who brought it,' he is not liable in the apparent narration, like his prevention after his saying, 'Whoever comes to you and describes such-and-such a sign, give it to him,' and a man described that sign and he did not give it to him until it was lost – he is not liable. And his prevention of the deposit of his slave from him is not oppression because the master does not have the right to take the deposit of his slave, whether the slave is permitted or interdicted, as long as he [the slave] does not present and it does not appear that it is from his earnings – due to the possibility that it is the property of someone else as a deposit. When it appears by evidence that it belongs to the slave, then he takes it; thus in al-Khulasa.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings on the depositary's liability when he refuses to return the deposit. The commentary expands on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The first ruling: if the owner asks for his deposit and the depositary says 'ask tomorrow,' and then the deposit is lost, liability depends on when the loss occurred. If the loss is after the acknowledgment (that he will return it tomorrow), he is not liable because the acknowledgment is a promise to return, not a guarantee. If the loss is before that, he is liable because he delayed without excuse. The second ruling: if the depositary says 'carry it today' and the day passes without him carrying it, he is not liable because the cost of returning is on the owner; the depositary is not obliged to transport it at his own expense. The third ruling: if the depositary refuses to give the deposit to the owner's messenger unless the messenger brings a guarantor, and the deposit is lost, he is not liable in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah). Similarly, if the owner says 'give it to whoever describes a certain sign' and a man describes it but the depositary does not give it, and it is lost, he is not liable. The fourth ruling: preventing the deposit of a slave from the master is not considered wrongful (zulm) because the master has no right to seize the deposit of his slave – it might belong to someone else. Only if it is proven by evidence that it is the slave's earnings does the master take it. These rulings clarify the boundaries of the depositary's duty: he is not a guarantor for every delay or refusal; specific conditions determine liability.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadī'ah): A trust deposit; property left with another for safekeeping without compensation.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or destruction of the deposit.
الإقرار (al-iqrār): Acknowledgment; here, the depositary's statement to return the deposit tomorrow implies an acknowledgment of the deposit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a depositary says 'ask tomorrow' and the deposit is lost after that statement, is he liable?
A) Yes, because he delayed
B) No, because the loss occurred after his acknowledgment
C) Yes, because he refused to return immediately
D) No, because the owner should have insisted

Key Takeaway
A depositary's liability for loss after refusal is not automatic; it hinges on the timing of the loss relative to his acknowledgment and the reasonableness of the refusal.

Mixing the Deposit Without Permission: Liability and Ownership
Ruling on mixing and its consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a depositary mixes the deposit with his own property without permission, and identify the conditions that trigger liability and ownership.

Core Idea
Mixing without permission makes the depositary liable and owner, but the owner loses all rights.

When a depositary mixes the deposit with his own property such that the two become indistinguishable, a critical legal transformation occurs. The depositary becomes a consumer (mustahlik) and is deemed to have taken ownership, but he cannot benefit until he compensates. This lesson unpacks the conditions and consequences of this mixing, including the effect of acquittal (ibrā') and the distinction between the depositary mixing and an outsider mixing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its trigger
The act is mixing (khalṭ) the deposit with the depositary's own property without permission, resulting in indistinguishability (lā tatamayyaz). This is the key condition for liability.
2
Determine the legal consequence
According to Abu Hanifa, the depositary becomes liable (ḍāmin), and upon liability he owns the deposit (yamlikuha). The owner has no recourse to the specific chattel, only to compensation.
Mixing with permission creates partnership, not liability.
3
Consider the effect of acquittal
If the owner acquits the depositary (ibrā'), his right to both the chattel and the debt falls. This absolves the depositary completely.
This applies only after mixing without permission.
4
Distinguish the mixer
The ruling applies only if the depositary himself mixed. If an outsider or family member mixed, the depositary is not liable; the mixer is liable.
The depositary remains a custodian for the untouched part.
5
Assess indistinguishability
The ruling applies only when mixing makes the property indistinguishable. If easy distinction is possible (e.g., walnuts with almonds), the owner's right continues.
Consensus (ijmā') that if distinguishable, no liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو خلطها بماله بغير الإذن حتى لا تتمير (ضمنها لأنه صار مستهلكاً لها وإذا ضمنها ملكها ولا تباح له قبل أداء الضمان ولا سبيل للمالك عليها عند أبي حنيفة، ولو أبرأه سقط حقه من العين والدين . أطلق المصنف فشمل خلط الجنس بجنسه أو بغير جنسه كخلط هو الزيت بالشيرج، والحنطة بالشعير وبالحنطة والفضة بالفضة بعد الإذابة . قيد بكون المودع . الخالط لأن الخالط لو كان أجنبياً أو من في عياله لا يضمن المودع والضمان على الخالط ولو الساعة فتركها وذهب ؛ إن تركها عن رضا فهلكت لا يضمن لأنه لما ذهب فقد أنشأ الوديعة، وإن كان عن غير رضا يضمن، ولو كان الذي طلب الوديعة وكيل المالك يضمن لأنه ليس له إنشاء الوديعة بخلاف المالك اهـ . وهذا صريح في أنه يضمن بعدم الدفع إلى وكيل المالك كما لا يخفى . وفي العمادية معزياً إلى الظهيرية : ورسول المودع إذا طلب الوديعة فقال لا أدفع إلا للذي جاء بها ولم يدفع إلى الرسول حتى هلكت ضمن. وذكر في فتاوى قاضي ظهير هذه المسألة، وأجاب نجم الدين أنه يضمن وفيه نظر بدليل أن المودع إذا صدق من ادعى أنه وكيل بقبض الوديعة فإنه قال في الوكالة : لا يؤمر بدفع الوديعة إليه ولكن لقائل أن يفرق بين الوكيل والرسول لأن الرسول ينطق على لسان المرسل ولا كذلك الوكيل ألا ترى أنه لو عزل الوكيل قبل علم الوكيل بالعزل لا يصح ، رجع عن الرسالة قبل علم الرسول بالرجوع صح؛ كذا في فتاواه اهـ. أقول: ظاهر ما نقله في الفصول العمادية معزياً إلى قاضي ظهير أنه لا يضمن في مسألة الوكيل كما هو منقول عن التجنيس فهو مخالف لما في الخلاصة كما هو ظاهره، ويتراءى لي التوفيق بين القولين بأن يحمل ما في الخلاصة على ما إذا قصد الوكيل إنشاء الوديعة عند المودع بعد منعه ليدفع له في وقت آخر، وما في فتاوى قاضي ظهير والتجنيس على ما إذا منع ليؤدي إلى المودع بنفسه ولذلك قال في جوابه : إلا للذي جاء بها. وفي الخلاصة ما هو صريح في أن الوكيل تركها وذهب عن رضا بعد قول المودع لا يمكنني أن أحضرها الساعة أي وأدفعها لك في غير هذه الساعة فإذا فارقه فقد أنشأ الإيداع وليس له ذلك بخلاف قوله لا أدفعها إلا للذي جاء بها فإنه استبقاء للإيداع الأول لا إنشاء إيداع فتأمل. ولم أر من تعرض لهذا التوفيق والله تعالى هو الموفق .اهـ. قوله : ( فإن قال ضاعت بعد الإقرار) أي الإقرار ضمناً في قوله أطلبها غداً وقوله بعد الإقرار ظرف لضاعت لا لـ«قال وفي جامع الفصولين: طلبها  ٤٧٠ كتاب الوديعة اشتركا ولو أنفق بعضها فرد مثله فخلطه بالباقي ضمن الكل وإن تعدى فيها ثم أزال صغيراً كان أو كبيراً، ولا يضمن أبوه لأجله ؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وقيد بكونها لا تتميز لأنه لو كان يمكن الوصول إليه على وجه التيسير كخلط الجوز باللوز والدراهم السود بالبيض فإنه لا ينقطع حق المالك إجماعاً، واستفيذ منه أن المراد بعدم التميز عدمه على وجه التيسير لا عدم إمكانه مطلقاً كما لا يخفى وإن خلطها بإذنه كان شريكاً له قوله : (وإن اختلط بغير فعله اشتركا) يعني وكانت شركة ملك ولا ضمان عليه لعدم الصنع منه فإن هلك بعضها هلك من مالهما جميعاً ويقسم الباقي بينهما على قدر ما كان لكل واحد منهما كالمال المشترك قوله : (ولو أنفق بعضها فرد مثله فخلطه بالباقي ضمن (الكل أي البعض بالإنفاق والبعض بالخلط لأنه متعد بالإنفاق منها ورد مثله باقٍ على ملكه وقد خلطه بما بقي من الوديعة فضمن الجميع . والمراد بالخلط هنا خلط لا تتميز معه، أما لو جعل عل ماله علامة حين خلطه بها بحيث يتأتى التمييز لا يضمن إلا ما أنفق؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وقيد بالإنفاق ورد المثل لأنه إذا أخذ بعض الوديعة لينفقه في حاجته فرده إلى موضعه ثم ضاعت الوديعة فلا ضمان عليه لوجهين : الأول أن رفعه حفظ فلا يضمن به ولا بمجرد النية الثاني أنه وإن صار ضامناً بالرفع فقد عاد إلى الوفاق برد العين إلى مكانها فبرئ عن الضمان بخلاف ما إذا رد مثله لأنه إنما جاء بملك نفسه فلا يكون عوداً إلى الوفاق وهو أولى من الأول، فإنهم قالوا بأنه لو باعها وضمن قيمتها نفذ البيع من جهته واستند ملكه بالضمان إلى وقت وجوب الضمان فلو لم يكن الرفع للبيع موجباً للضمان عليه قبل البيع والتسليم لم يستند ملكه إلى تلك الحالة؛ كذا في النهاية. وقيد بقوله «فرد مثلها لأنه لو لم يرد كان ضامناً لما أنفق خاصة لأنه حافظ للباقي ولم يتعيب لأنه مما لا يضره التبعيض لأن الكلام فيما إذا كانت الوديعة دراهم أو دنانير أو أشياء من المكيل والموزون فهو كما لو أودعه وديعتين فأنفق إحداهما لا يكون ضامناً للأخرى؛ كذا في النهاية قوله : (وإن تعدى فيها ثم أزال التعدي زال الضمان أي تعدى في الوديعة بأن كانت دابة فركبها أو ثوباً فلبسه أو عبداً فاستخدمه أو أودعها غيره ثم أزال التعدي فردها إلى يده برئ عن الضمان لأنه مأمور بالحفظ في كل الأوقات، فإذا خالف في البعض ثم رجع أتى بالمأمور به كما إذا استأجره للحفظ شهراً فترك الحفظ في بعضه ثم حفظ في الباقي استحق ربها فقال أطلبها غداً فقال في الغد تلفت قبل قولي أطلبها غداً ضمن التناقضه لا بعده الهـ. والمسألة في الخانية أيضاً. قوله : (وقيدنا بكونه نقلها الخ) (فش) : جحدها فلو نقلها من مكان كانت فيه حال الجحود ضمن وإلا فلا فلو قلنا بوجوب الضمان في الوجهين فله وجه خلاصة لو جحدها إنما يضمن إذا نقلها عن موضعها التي كانت فيه حال الجحود وهلكت، وإن لم ينقلها وهلكت لا يضمن. وفي
His saying: (Or he mixed it with his property without permission such that it cannot be distinguished [then he is liable]) because he became a consumer of it. And when he becomes liable, he owns it, and it is not permissible for him before paying the liability, and the owner has no recourse to the chattel according to Abu Hanifa. And if he [the owner] acquits him, his right to the chattel and debt falls. The author made it general, so it includes mixing a species with its species or with another species, like mixing oil with sesame oil, wheat with barley or with wheat, and silver with silver after melting. It is restricted to the depositary being the mixer, because if the mixer were an outsider or someone in his household, the depositary is not liable; liability is on the mixer. And if he [the owner] said, 'I cannot bring it now,' so he [the depositary] left it and went; if he left it with consent, and it perished, he is not liable because when he went, he established a deposit; if without consent, he is liable. And if the one who asked for the deposit was the owner's agent, he is liable because he does not have the right to establish a deposit, unlike the owner. This is explicit that he is liable for not handing over to the owner's agent. In al-'Imadiyah quoting al-Zahiriyah: The depositary's messenger if he asked for the deposit and he said, 'I will not hand over except to the one who brought it,' and he did not hand over to the messenger until it perished – he is liable. And it is mentioned in the Fatawa of Qadi Zahir this issue, and Najm al-Din answered that he is liable, and there is consideration in it, because the depositary if he believes someone who claims to be an agent for receiving the deposit, then it is said in the book of agency: he is not ordered to hand over the deposit to him. But it can be said there is a difference between the agent and the messenger because the messenger speaks on the tongue of the sender and not so the agent. Do you not see that if the agent is dismissed before the agent knows of the dismissal, it is not valid, but if he withdraws from the message before the messenger knows of the withdrawal, it is valid? Thus in his Fatawa. I say: The apparent meaning of what is transmitted in al-Fusul al-'Imadiyah quoting Qadi Zahir is that he is not liable in the issue of the agent, as is transmitted from al-Tajnīs. This contradicts what is in al-Khulasa as is apparent. And it seems to me that reconciliation between the two sayings is that what is in al-Khulasa is for when the agent intended to establish a deposit with the depositary after his refusal so that he would hand over at another time, and what is in the Fatawa of Qadi Zahir and al-Tajnīs is for when he refused to hand over to the agent so that he himself would deliver to the owner. Therefore he said in his answer: except to the one who brought it. In al-Khulasa it is explicit that the agent left it and went with consent after the depositary said, 'I cannot bring it now,' meaning I will hand it over to you at another time, so when he parted company with him, he established the deposit, and he does not have that right, unlike his saying, 'I will not hand it over except to the one who brought it,' because that is a continuation of the first deposit, not an establishment of a new deposit. So ponder. I have not seen anyone who addressed this reconciliation, and Allah is the Granter of success. His saying: (So if he said 'It was lost after the acknowledgment') i.e., the acknowledgment implicitly in his saying 'Ask for it tomorrow,' and his saying 'after the acknowledgment' is an adverb for 'was lost' not for 'said.' And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: He asked for it [PAGE_470] 470 Book of Deposit They share, and if he spent some of it, then replaced its like and mixed it with the remainder, he is liable for the whole. And if he transgressed in it then removed [the transgression], whether small or large, and his father is not liable on his account; thus in al-Khulasa. And it is restricted to it being indistinguishable, because if it could be reached with ease, like mixing walnuts with almonds and black dirhams with white, then the owner's right is not cut off by consensus. And it is understood from this that the meaning of indistinguishability is the lack of it with ease, not the absolute impossibility, as is clear. And if he mixed it with his permission, he is his partner. His saying: (And if it became mixed without his action, they share) meaning it is a partnership of ownership, and there is no liability on him because there is no action from him. If some of it perishes, it perishes from both their property, and the remainder is divided between them according to what each one had, like joint property. His saying: (And if he spent some of it then replaced its like and mixed it with the remainder, he is liable for the whole) i.e., part by spending and part by mixing, because he transgressed by spending from it, and the like he returned remains in his ownership, and he mixed it with what remained of the deposit, so he is liable for all. And what is meant by mixing here is mixing that renders it indistinguishable. As for if he placed a mark on his property while mixing it such that distinction is possible, he is only liable for what he spent; thus in al-Khulasa. And it is restricted by spending and returning the like, because if he took some of the deposit to spend on his need and returned it to its place, then the deposit was lost, there is no liability on him for two reasons: first, his taking was for preservation, so he is not liable by it nor by mere intention; second, even if he became liable by taking, he returned to compliance by returning the chattel to its place, so he is free from liability, unlike if he returned its like, because he only brought his own property, so it is not a return to compliance. And this is more appropriate than the first, because they said that if he sold it and paid its value, the sale was effective on his part, and his ownership by liability was traced back to the time liability became due. If the taking for sale had not necessitated liability before the sale and delivery, his ownership would not be traced back to that state; thus in al-Nihaya. And it is restricted by his saying 'returned its like' because if he did not return, he would be liable only for what he spent, because he is the preserver of the remainder and it is not defective because partial loss does not harm it, since the discussion is when the deposit is dirhams or dinars or things from weighed or measured items. So it is as if he deposited two deposits with him and he spent one of them - he is not liable for the other; thus in al-Nihaya. His saying: (And if he transgressed in it then removed the transgression, liability is removed) i.e., he transgressed in the deposit, like if it was an animal and he rode it, or a garment and he wore it, or a slave and he employed him, or gave it to another as a deposit, then he removed the transgression and returned it to his hand, he is free from liability because he was commanded to preserve it at all times. So when he violated in part and then returned, he performed what he was commanded to do, just as if one hired him to preserve for a month, he left preservation for part of it then preserved for the remainder, he deserves [the wage]. Its owner said, 'Ask for it tomorrow,' so he said the next day, 'It was lost before my saying 'ask for it tomorrow'' - he is liable due to contradiction, not after. And the issue is in al-Khaniyyah as well. His saying: (And we restricted it by his moving it etc.) (fash): He denied it, so if he moved it from its place that it was in during the denial, he is liable, otherwise not. If we say obligation of liability in both cases, then there is a point. In al-Khulasa: If he denied it, he is only liable if he moved it from its place that it was in during the denial and it perished; if he did not move it and it perished, he is not liable.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This lengthy passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq covers several interrelated rulings about the depositary's liability when he mixes the deposit with his own property, spends part of it, or commits transgression (ta'addi). The passage type is a collection of rulings and intra-Hanafi nuances. The author (Ibn Nujaym) expands on the matn and reconciles conflicting narrations.

First ruling: Mixing the deposit with one's own property without permission such that it becomes indistinguishable (لا تتميز) makes the depositary liable. According to Abu Hanifa, liability transfers ownership of the deposit to the depositary, but he may not benefit from it until he pays compensation, and the owner has no recourse to the original chattel. If the owner acquits him (ibrā'), his right to both the chattel and the debt falls. Mixing includes both same genus and different genus (e.g., oil with sesame oil, wheat with barley, silver after melting). The mixing must be by the depositary himself; if an outsider or a family member mixes, the depositary is not liable, the mixer is.

Second ruling: If the owner says 'I cannot bring it now' and leaves the deposit with the depositary, then if the depositary leaves it with the owner's consent and it perishes, he is not liable because the owner has re-established the deposit. Without consent, he is liable.

Third ruling: If the agent of the owner requests the deposit and the depositary refuses to hand over, he is liable because the agent cannot create a new deposit. The commentary then discusses a difference of opinion between al-Khulasa and Fatawa Qadi Zahir/al-Tajnis: when the agent intends to establish a new deposit (i.e., leave it for later), the depositary is liable; when the depositary merely refuses to hand over to the agent (wanting to give to the owner directly), he is not liable. The author reconciles the two positions.

Fourth ruling (page 470): If the deposit becomes mixed without the depositary's action (e.g., accidentally), there is no liability and they become co-owners (shirkat milk). If part perishes, it is from both proportionately.

Fifth ruling: If the depositary spends part of the deposit and then returns an equivalent (mithl) from his own property and mixes it with the remaining deposit, he is liable for the whole (both for the spending and the mixing that makes it indistinguishable). However, if he spends and then returns the very same chattel (ayn) to its place, he is not liable because it is a return to compliance. The distinction is crucial: returning the 'ayn restores the trust; returning a substitute does not, because the substitute is his own property, not the original deposit.

Sixth ruling: If the depositary commits any transgression (e.g., riding a deposited animal, wearing a garment, employing a slave, re-depositing with another) and then removes the transgression (returns the item to its proper state), liability is removed. The transgression is considered a temporary breach; once corrected, the depositary returns to being a trusted custodian.

Finally, the passage includes a note about denial (juhud): if the depositary denies the deposit and moves it from its place at the time of denial, he is liable; if he does not move it, he is not liable upon loss.

Throughout, the authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah where mentioned (e.g., 'on the apparent narration'), and the mufta bihi positions follow the main rulings.

Key Terms
الخلط (al-khalṭ): Mixing the deposited property with the depositary's own property such that they become indistinguishable.
إبراء (ibrā'): Acquittal; the owner's waiver of all claims related to the deposit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a depositary mixes the deposit with his own property without permission and the mixture becomes indistinguishable, what is the immediate legal effect according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The depositary becomes a trustee and must return the same property
B) The depositary becomes liable and owner of the mixture
C) The deposit becomes joint property (shirkat milk)
D) The depositary must seek the owner's permission to continue

Key Takeaway
Mixing the deposit without permission causing indistinguishability makes the depositary liable and transfers ownership; acquittal extinguishes all claims.

intermediate
12 min
Spending Part of the Deposit: Liability for Replacement vs. Original
Ruling on spending and returning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling when a depositary spends part of the deposit and returns either the original chattel (ayn) or an equivalent (mithl), and explain why returning the equivalent does not remove liability.

Core Idea
Returning the original chattel after spending removes liability; returning a substitute does not because it is own property.

If a depositary takes some of the deposit for personal use and later returns either the exact item or a similar one, the liability depends on what was returned. Returning the original (ayn) restores the trust; returning an equivalent (mithl) does not – the depositary remains liable for the whole. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the importance of the 'ayn vs. mithl distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of spending
The depositary spends (anfaqa) part of the deposit – e.g., takes some dirhams to buy something.
This is a transgression (ta'addī) until rectified.
2
Distinguish two scenarios
Scenario A: Depositary returns the exact same item (ayn) to its place. Scenario B: Depositary returns an equivalent (mithl) from his own property and mixes it with the remainder.
The ruling differs based on what is returned.
3
Analyze Scenario A
If he returns the original, he is not liable for subsequent loss of the whole because (1) his taking was for preservation, and (2) he returned to compliance (wafāq).
The depositary is still a custodian; returning the ayn removes the transgression.
4
Analyze Scenario B
If he returns a mithl and mixes it with the remaining deposit, he is liable for the whole: part because he spent (transgression), part because the mixing without distinction makes him liable for the remainder.
The mithl is his own property; mixing it does not restore the trust.
5
Understand the subtlety
If the depositary sells the deposit and pays its value, the sale is effective and his ownership traces back to the time of liability. This shows the importance of the original ayn.
This is from the commentary citing al-Nihaya.
