لـ«قال وفي جامع الفصولين: طلبها  ٤٧٠ كتاب الوديعة اشتركا ولو أنفق بعضها فرد مثله فخلطه بالباقي ضمن الكل وإن تعدى فيها ثم أزال صغيراً كان أو كبيراً، ولا يضمن أبوه لأجله ؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وقيد بكونها لا تتميز لأنه لو كان يمكن الوصول إليه على وجه التيسير كخلط الجوز باللوز والدراهم السود بالبيض فإنه لا ينقطع حق المالك إجماعاً، واستفيذ منه أن المراد بعدم التميز عدمه على وجه التيسير لا عدم إمكانه مطلقاً كما لا يخفى وإن خلطها بإذنه كان شريكاً له قوله : (وإن اختلط بغير فعله اشتركا) يعني وكانت شركة ملك ولا ضمان عليه لعدم الصنع منه فإن هلك بعضها هلك من مالهما جميعاً ويقسم الباقي بينهما على قدر ما كان لكل واحد منهما كالمال المشترك قوله : (ولو أنفق بعضها فرد مثله فخلطه بالباقي ضمن (الكل أي البعض بالإنفاق والبعض بالخلط لأنه متعد بالإنفاق منها ورد مثله باقٍ على ملكه وقد خلطه بما بقي من الوديعة فضمن الجميع . والمراد بالخلط هنا خلط لا تتميز معه، أما لو جعل عل ماله علامة حين خلطه بها بحيث يتأتى التمييز لا يضمن إلا ما أنفق؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وقيد بالإنفاق ورد المثل لأنه إذا أخذ بعض الوديعة لينفقه في حاجته فرده إلى موضعه ثم ضاعت الوديعة فلا ضمان عليه لوجهين : الأول أن رفعه حفظ فلا يضمن به ولا بمجرد النية الثاني أنه وإن صار ضامناً بالرفع فقد عاد إلى الوفاق برد العين إلى مكانها فبرئ عن الضمان بخلاف ما إذا رد مثله لأنه إنما جاء بملك نفسه فلا يكون عوداً إلى الوفاق وهو أولى من الأول، فإنهم قالوا بأنه لو باعها وضمن قيمتها نفذ البيع من جهته واستند ملكه بالضمان إلى وقت وجوب الضمان فلو لم يكن الرفع للبيع موجباً للضمان عليه قبل البيع والتسليم لم يستند ملكه إلى تلك الحالة؛ كذا في النهاية. وقيد بقوله «فرد مثلها لأنه لو لم يرد كان ضامناً لما أنفق خاصة لأنه حافظ للباقي ولم يتعيب لأنه مما لا يضره التبعيض لأن الكلام فيما إذا كانت الوديعة دراهم أو دنانير أو أشياء من المكيل والموزون فهو كما لو أودعه وديعتين فأنفق إحداهما لا يكون ضامناً للأخرى؛ كذا في النهاية قوله : (وإن تعدى فيها ثم أزال التعدي زال الضمان أي تعدى في الوديعة بأن كانت دابة فركبها أو ثوباً فلبسه أو عبداً فاستخدمه أو أودعها غيره ثم أزال التعدي فردها إلى يده برئ عن الضمان لأنه مأمور بالحفظ في كل الأوقات، فإذا خالف في البعض ثم رجع أتى بالمأمور به كما إذا استأجره للحفظ شهراً فترك الحفظ في بعضه ثم حفظ في الباقي استحق ربها فقال أطلبها غداً فقال في الغد تلفت قبل قولي أطلبها غداً ضمن التناقضه لا بعده الهـ. والمسألة في الخانية أيضاً. قوله : (وقيدنا بكونه نقلها الخ) (فش) : جحدها فلو نقلها من مكان كانت فيه حال الجحود ضمن وإلا فلا فلو قلنا بوجوب الضمان في الوجهين فله وجه خلاصة لو جحدها إنما يضمن إذا نقلها عن موضعها التي كانت فيه حال الجحود وهلكت، وإن لم ينقلها وهلكت لا يضمن. وفي
His saying: (Or he mixed it with his property without permission such that it cannot be distinguished [then he is liable]) because he became a consumer of it. And when he becomes liable, he owns it, and it is not permissible for him before paying the liability, and the owner has no recourse to the chattel according to Abu Hanifa. And if he [the owner] acquits him, his right to the chattel and debt falls. The author made it general, so it includes mixing a species with its species or with another species, like mixing oil with sesame oil, wheat with barley or with wheat, and silver with silver after melting. It is restricted to the depositary being the mixer, because if the mixer were an outsider or someone in his household, the depositary is not liable; liability is on the mixer. And if he [the owner] said, 'I cannot bring it now,' so he [the depositary] left it and went; if he left it with consent, and it perished, he is not liable because when he went, he established a deposit; if without consent, he is liable. And if the one who asked for the deposit was the owner's agent, he is liable because he does not have the right to establish a deposit, unlike the owner. This is explicit that he is liable for not handing over to the owner's agent. In al-'Imadiyah quoting al-Zahiriyah: The depositary's messenger if he asked for the deposit and he said, 'I will not hand over except to the one who brought it,' and he did not hand over to the messenger until it perished – he is liable. And it is mentioned in the Fatawa of Qadi Zahir this issue, and Najm al-Din answered that he is liable, and there is consideration in it, because the depositary if he believes someone who claims to be an agent for receiving the deposit, then it is said in the book of agency: he is not ordered to hand over the deposit to him. But it can be said there is a difference between the agent and the messenger because the messenger speaks on the tongue of the sender and not so the agent. Do you not see that if the agent is dismissed before the agent knows of the dismissal, it is not valid, but if he withdraws from the message before the messenger knows of the withdrawal, it is valid? Thus in his Fatawa. I say: The apparent meaning of what is transmitted in al-Fusul al-'Imadiyah quoting Qadi Zahir is that he is not liable in the issue of the agent, as is transmitted from al-Tajnīs. This contradicts what is in al-Khulasa as is apparent. And it seems to me that reconciliation between the two sayings is that what is in al-Khulasa is for when the agent intended to establish a deposit with the depositary after his refusal so that he would hand over at another time, and what is in the Fatawa of Qadi Zahir and al-Tajnīs is for when he refused to hand over to the agent so that he himself would deliver to the owner. Therefore he said in his answer: except to the one who brought it. In al-Khulasa it is explicit that the agent left it and went with consent after the depositary said, 'I cannot bring it now,' meaning I will hand it over to you at another time, so when he parted company with him, he established the deposit, and he does not have that right, unlike his saying, 'I will not hand it over except to the one who brought it,' because that is a continuation of the first deposit, not an establishment of a new deposit. So ponder. I have not seen anyone who addressed this reconciliation, and Allah is the Granter of success. His saying: (So if he said 'It was lost after the acknowledgment') i.e., the acknowledgment implicitly in his saying 'Ask for it tomorrow,' and his saying 'after the acknowledgment' is an adverb for 'was lost' not for 'said.' And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: He asked for it [PAGE_470] 470 Book of Deposit They share, and if he spent some of it, then replaced its like and mixed it with the remainder, he is liable for the whole. And if he transgressed in it then removed [the transgression], whether small or large, and his father is not liable on his account; thus in al-Khulasa. And it is restricted to it being indistinguishable, because if it could be reached with ease, like mixing walnuts with almonds and black dirhams with white, then the owner's right is not cut off by consensus. And it is understood from this that the meaning of indistinguishability is the lack of it with ease, not the absolute impossibility, as is clear. And if he mixed it with his permission, he is his partner. His saying: (And if it became mixed without his action, they share) meaning it is a partnership of ownership, and there is no liability on him because there is no action from him. If some of it perishes, it perishes from both their property, and the remainder is divided between them according to what each one had, like joint property. His saying: (And if he spent some of it then replaced its like and mixed it with the remainder, he is liable for the whole) i.e., part by spending and part by mixing, because he transgressed by spending from it, and the like he returned remains in his ownership, and he mixed it with what remained of the deposit, so he is liable for all. And what is meant by mixing here is mixing that renders it indistinguishable. As for if he placed a mark on his property while mixing it such that distinction is possible, he is only liable for what he spent; thus in al-Khulasa. And it is restricted by spending and returning the like, because if he took some of the deposit to spend on his need and returned it to its place, then the deposit was lost, there is no liability on him for two reasons: first, his taking was for preservation, so he is not liable by it nor by mere intention; second, even if he became liable by taking, he returned to compliance by returning the chattel to its place, so he is free from liability, unlike if he returned its like, because he only brought his own property, so it is not a return to compliance. And this is more appropriate than the first, because they said that if he sold it and paid its value, the sale was effective on his part, and his ownership by liability was traced back to the time liability became due. If the taking for sale had not necessitated liability before the sale and delivery, his ownership would not be traced back to that state; thus in al-Nihaya. And it is restricted by his saying 'returned its like' because if he did not return, he would be liable only for what he spent, because he is the preserver of the remainder and it is not defective because partial loss does not harm it, since the discussion is when the deposit is dirhams or dinars or things from weighed or measured items. So it is as if he deposited two deposits with him and he spent one of them - he is not liable for the other; thus in al-Nihaya. His saying: (And if he transgressed in it then removed the transgression, liability is removed) i.e., he transgressed in the deposit, like if it was an animal and he rode it, or a garment and he wore it, or a slave and he employed him, or gave it to another as a deposit, then he removed the transgression and returned it to his hand, he is free from liability because he was commanded to preserve it at all times. So when he violated in part and then returned, he performed what he was commanded to do, just as if one hired him to preserve for a month, he left preservation for part of it then preserved for the remainder, he deserves [the wage]. Its owner said, 'Ask for it tomorrow,' so he said the next day, 'It was lost before my saying 'ask for it tomorrow'' - he is liable due to contradiction, not after. And the issue is in al-Khaniyyah as well. His saying: (And we restricted it by his moving it etc.) (fash): He denied it, so if he moved it from its place that it was in during the denial, he is liable, otherwise not. If we say obligation of liability in both cases, then there is a point. In al-Khulasa: If he denied it, he is only liable if he moved it from its place that it was in during the denial and it perished; if he did not move it and it perished, he is not liable.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This lengthy passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq covers several interrelated rulings about the depositary's liability when he mixes the deposit with his own property, spends part of it, or commits transgression (ta'addi). The passage type is a collection of rulings and intra-Hanafi nuances. The author (Ibn Nujaym) expands on the matn and reconciles conflicting narrations.

First ruling: Mixing the deposit with one's own property without permission such that it becomes indistinguishable (لا تتميز) makes the depositary liable. According to Abu Hanifa, liability transfers ownership of the deposit to the depositary, but he may not benefit from it until he pays compensation, and the owner has no recourse to the original chattel. If the owner acquits him (ibrā'), his right to both the chattel and the debt falls. Mixing includes both same genus and different genus (e.g., oil with sesame oil, wheat with barley, silver after melting). The mixing must be by the depositary himself; if an outsider or a family member mixes, the depositary is not liable, the mixer is.

Second ruling: If the owner says 'I cannot bring it now' and leaves the deposit with the depositary, then if the depositary leaves it with the owner's consent and it perishes, he is not liable because the owner has re-established the deposit. Without consent, he is liable.

Third ruling: If the agent of the owner requests the deposit and the depositary refuses to hand over, he is liable because the agent cannot create a new deposit. The commentary then discusses a difference of opinion between al-Khulasa and Fatawa Qadi Zahir/al-Tajnis: when the agent intends to establish a new deposit (i.e., leave it for later), the depositary is liable; when the depositary merely refuses to hand over to the agent (wanting to give to the owner directly), he is not liable. The author reconciles the two positions.

Fourth ruling (page 470): If the deposit becomes mixed without the depositary's action (e.g., accidentally), there is no liability and they become co-owners (shirkat milk). If part perishes, it is from both proportionately.

Fifth ruling: If the depositary spends part of the deposit and then returns an equivalent (mithl) from his own property and mixes it with the remaining deposit, he is liable for the whole (both for the spending and the mixing that makes it indistinguishable). However, if he spends and then returns the very same chattel (ayn) to its place, he is not liable because it is a return to compliance. The distinction is crucial: returning the 'ayn restores the trust; returning a substitute does not, because the substitute is his own property, not the original deposit.

Sixth ruling: If the depositary commits any transgression (e.g., riding a deposited animal, wearing a garment, employing a slave, re-depositing with another) and then removes the transgression (returns the item to its proper state), liability is removed. The transgression is considered a temporary breach; once corrected, the depositary returns to being a trusted custodian.

Finally, the passage includes a note about denial (juhud): if the depositary denies the deposit and moves it from its place at the time of denial, he is liable; if he does not move it, he is not liable upon loss.

Throughout, the authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah where mentioned (e.g., 'on the apparent narration'), and the mufta bihi positions follow the main rulings.

Key Terms
العين (al-'ayn): The specific physical chattel originally deposited.
المثل (al-mithl): An equivalent in quantity and quality from the depositary's own property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A depositary takes 5 kg of deposited rice, uses it, then returns 5 kg of his own rice but mixes it with the remaining 15 kg such that they are indistinguishable. The whole 20 kg is then lost. What is the depositary's liability?
A) No liability because he returned the same quantity
B) Liable for 5 kg only
C) Liable for the whole 20 kg
D) Liable for 15 kg only

Key Takeaway
Returning the original chattel after spending removes liability; returning a substitute and mixing it creates liability for the whole.

beginner
8 min
Removal of Transgression (Ta'addī): Liability and Restoration
Ruling on removal of transgression

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that removing a transgression (ta'addī) in the deposit restores the depositary's protection and removes liability.

Core Idea
A transgression is wiped clean when the depositary returns the deposit to its proper state.

If a depositary uses the deposit in an unauthorized manner (e.g., riding a horse, wearing a garment), he commits transgression (ta'addī) and becomes liable. However, if he voluntarily ceases the transgression and returns the deposit to its original condition, the liability is removed. This lesson explores the conditions for removal, including the unusual case of denial (juhūd) and moving the deposit.

Guided Path
1
Define transgression (ta'addī)
Any unauthorized use of the deposit: riding a deposited animal, wearing a garment, employing a slave, or re-depositing with another person.
Ta'addī triggers liability for loss even without fault.
2
Identify the act of removal
The depositary must actively remove the transgression – e.g., stop riding, return the garment to the trunk, retrieve the deposit from the third party.
Mere intention is not enough; action is required.
3
Confirm removal eliminates liability
Once the transgression is removed, the depositary returns to his role as a trustee (amīn) and is no longer liable for subsequent loss unless a new transgression occurs.
This is based on the principle of returning to compliance (wafāq).
4
Distinguish the case of denial (juhūd)
If the depositary denies the deposit (juhūd) and does not move it, he is not liable. If he moves it while denying, he becomes liable.
Denial alone is a sin but does not create liability unless accompanied by a change of possession.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi nuance on moving
There is a view that any denial makes the depositary liable even without moving, but the stronger view is that moving is required.
The passage mentions a point in al-Khulasa.

لأن الكلام فيما إذا كانت الوديعة دراهم أو دنانير أو أشياء من المكيل والموزون فهو كما لو أودعه وديعتين فأنفق إحداهما لا يكون ضامناً للأخرى؛ كذا في النهاية قوله : (وإن تعدى فيها ثم أزال التعدي زال بالمأمور به كما إذا استأجره للحفظ شهراً فترك الحفظ في بعضه ثم حفظ في الباقي استحق ربها فقال أطلبها غداً فقال في الغد تلفت قبل قولي أطلبها غداً ضمن التناقضه لا بعده الهـ. والمسألة في الخانية أيضاً. قوله : (وقيدنا بكونه نقلها الخ) (فش) : جحدها فلو نقلها من مكان كانت فيه حال الجحود ضمن وإلا فلا فلو قلنا بوجوب الضمان في الوجهين فله وجه خلاصة لو جحدها إنما يضمن إذا نقلها عن موضعها التي كانت فيه حال الجحود وهلكت، وإن لم ينقلها وهلكت لا يضمن. وفي
His saying: (Or he mixed it with his property without permission such that it cannot be distinguished [then he is liable]) because he became a consumer of it. And when he becomes liable, he owns it, and it is not permissible for him before paying the liability, and the owner has no recourse to the chattel according to Abu Hanifa. And if he [the owner] acquits him, his right to the chattel and debt falls. The author made it general, so it includes mixing a species with its species or with another species, like mixing oil with sesame oil, wheat with barley or with wheat, and silver with silver after melting. It is restricted to the depositary being the mixer, because if the mixer were an outsider or someone in his household, the depositary is not liable; liability is on the mixer. And if he [the owner] said, 'I cannot bring it now,' so he [the depositary] left it and went; if he left it with consent, and it perished, he is not liable because when he went, he established a deposit; if without consent, he is liable. And if the one who asked for the deposit was the owner's agent, he is liable because he does not have the right to establish a deposit, unlike the owner. This is explicit that he is liable for not handing over to the owner's agent. In al-'Imadiyah quoting al-Zahiriyah: The depositary's messenger if he asked for the deposit and he said, 'I will not hand over except to the one who brought it,' and he did not hand over to the messenger until it perished – he is liable. And it is mentioned in the Fatawa of Qadi Zahir this issue, and Najm al-Din answered that he is liable, and there is consideration in it, because the depositary if he believes someone who claims to be an agent for receiving the deposit, then it is said in the book of agency: he is not ordered to hand over the deposit to him. But it can be said there is a difference between the agent and the messenger because the messenger speaks on the tongue of the sender and not so the agent. Do you not see that if the agent is dismissed before the agent knows of the dismissal, it is not valid, but if he withdraws from the message before the messenger knows of the withdrawal, it is valid? Thus in his Fatawa. I say: The apparent meaning of what is transmitted in al-Fusul al-'Imadiyah quoting Qadi Zahir is that he is not liable in the issue of the agent, as is transmitted from al-Tajnīs. This contradicts what is in al-Khulasa as is apparent. And it seems to me that reconciliation between the two sayings is that what is in al-Khulasa is for when the agent intended to establish a deposit with the depositary after his refusal so that he would hand over at another time, and what is in the Fatawa of Qadi Zahir and al-Tajnīs is for when he refused to hand over to the agent so that he himself would deliver to the owner. Therefore he said in his answer: except to the one who brought it. In al-Khulasa it is explicit that the agent left it and went with consent after the depositary said, 'I cannot bring it now,' meaning I will hand it over to you at another time, so when he parted company with him, he established the deposit, and he does not have that right, unlike his saying, 'I will not hand it over except to the one who brought it,' because that is a continuation of the first deposit, not an establishment of a new deposit. So ponder. I have not seen anyone who addressed this reconciliation, and Allah is the Granter of success. His saying: (So if he said 'It was lost after the acknowledgment') i.e., the acknowledgment implicitly in his saying 'Ask for it tomorrow,' and his saying 'after the acknowledgment' is an adverb for 'was lost' not for 'said.' And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: He asked for it [PAGE_470] 470 Book of Deposit They share, and if he spent some of it, then replaced its like and mixed it with the remainder, he is liable for the whole. And if he transgressed in it then removed [the transgression], whether small or large, and his father is not liable on his account; thus in al-Khulasa. And it is restricted to it being indistinguishable, because if it could be reached with ease, like mixing walnuts with almonds and black dirhams with white, then the owner's right is not cut off by consensus. And it is understood from this that the meaning of indistinguishability is the lack of it with ease, not the absolute impossibility, as is clear. And if he mixed it with his permission, he is his partner. His saying: (And if it became mixed without his action, they share) meaning it is a partnership of ownership, and there is no liability on him because there is no action from him. If some of it perishes, it perishes from both their property, and the remainder is divided between them according to what each one had, like joint property. His saying: (And if he spent some of it then replaced its like and mixed it with the remainder, he is liable for the whole) i.e., part by spending and part by mixing, because he transgressed by spending from it, and the like he returned remains in his ownership, and he mixed it with what remained of the deposit, so he is liable for all. And what is meant by mixing here is mixing that renders it indistinguishable. As for if he placed a mark on his property while mixing it such that distinction is possible, he is only liable for what he spent; thus in al-Khulasa. And it is restricted by spending and returning the like, because if he took some of the deposit to spend on his need and returned it to its place, then the deposit was lost, there is no liability on him for two reasons: first, his taking was for preservation, so he is not liable by it nor by mere intention; second, even if he became liable by taking, he returned to compliance by returning the chattel to its place, so he is free from liability, unlike if he returned its like, because he only brought his own property, so it is not a return to compliance. And this is more appropriate than the first, because they said that if he sold it and paid its value, the sale was effective on his part, and his ownership by liability was traced back to the time liability became due. If the taking for sale had not necessitated liability before the sale and delivery, his ownership would not be traced back to that state; thus in al-Nihaya. And it is restricted by his saying 'returned its like' because if he did not return, he would be liable only for what he spent, because he is the preserver of the remainder and it is not defective because partial loss does not harm it, since the discussion is when the deposit is dirhams or dinars or things from weighed or measured items. So it is as if he deposited two deposits with him and he spent one of them - he is not liable for the other; thus in al-Nihaya. His saying: (And if he transgressed in it then removed the transgression, liability is removed) i.e., he transgressed in the deposit, like if it was an animal and he rode it, or a garment and he wore it, or a slave and he employed him, or gave it to another as a deposit, then he removed the transgression and returned it to his hand, he is free from liability because he was commanded to preserve it at all times. So when he violated in part and then returned, he performed what he was commanded to do, just as if one hired him to preserve for a month, he left preservation for part of it then preserved for the remainder, he deserves [the wage]. Its owner said, 'Ask for it tomorrow,' so he said the next day, 'It was lost before my saying 'ask for it tomorrow'' - he is liable due to contradiction, not after. And the issue is in al-Khaniyyah as well. His saying: (And we restricted it by his moving it etc.) (fash): He denied it, so if he moved it from its place that it was in during the denial, he is liable, otherwise not. If we say obligation of liability in both cases, then there is a point. In al-Khulasa: If he denied it, he is only liable if he moved it from its place that it was in during the denial and it perished; if he did not move it and it perished, he is not liable.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This lengthy passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq covers several interrelated rulings about the depositary's liability when he mixes the deposit with his own property, spends part of it, or commits transgression (ta'addi). The passage type is a collection of rulings and intra-Hanafi nuances. The author (Ibn Nujaym) expands on the matn and reconciles conflicting narrations.

First ruling: Mixing the deposit with one's own property without permission such that it becomes indistinguishable (لا تتميز) makes the depositary liable. According to Abu Hanifa, liability transfers ownership of the deposit to the depositary, but he may not benefit from it until he pays compensation, and the owner has no recourse to the original chattel. If the owner acquits him (ibrā'), his right to both the chattel and the debt falls. Mixing includes both same genus and different genus (e.g., oil with sesame oil, wheat with barley, silver after melting). The mixing must be by the depositary himself; if an outsider or a family member mixes, the depositary is not liable, the mixer is.

Second ruling: If the owner says 'I cannot bring it now' and leaves the deposit with the depositary, then if the depositary leaves it with the owner's consent and it perishes, he is not liable because the owner has re-established the deposit. Without consent, he is liable.

Third ruling: If the agent of the owner requests the deposit and the depositary refuses to hand over, he is liable because the agent cannot create a new deposit. The commentary then discusses a difference of opinion between al-Khulasa and Fatawa Qadi Zahir/al-Tajnis: when the agent intends to establish a new deposit (i.e., leave it for later), the depositary is liable; when the depositary merely refuses to hand over to the agent (wanting to give to the owner directly), he is not liable. The author reconciles the two positions.

Fourth ruling (page 470): If the deposit becomes mixed without the depositary's action (e.g., accidentally), there is no liability and they become co-owners (shirkat milk). If part perishes, it is from both proportionately.

Fifth ruling: If the depositary spends part of the deposit and then returns an equivalent (mithl) from his own property and mixes it with the remaining deposit, he is liable for the whole (both for the spending and the mixing that makes it indistinguishable). However, if he spends and then returns the very same chattel (ayn) to its place, he is not liable because it is a return to compliance. The distinction is crucial: returning the 'ayn restores the trust; returning a substitute does not, because the substitute is his own property, not the original deposit.

Sixth ruling: If the depositary commits any transgression (e.g., riding a deposited animal, wearing a garment, employing a slave, re-depositing with another) and then removes the transgression (returns the item to its proper state), liability is removed. The transgression is considered a temporary breach; once corrected, the depositary returns to being a trusted custodian.

Finally, the passage includes a note about denial (juhud): if the depositary denies the deposit and moves it from its place at the time of denial, he is liable; if he does not move it, he is not liable upon loss.

Throughout, the authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah where mentioned (e.g., 'on the apparent narration'), and the mufta bihi positions follow the main rulings.

Key Terms
التعدي (al-ta'addī): Unauthorized use of the deposit that triggers liability.
الجحود (al-juhūd): Denial of the deposit; alone does not create liability unless accompanied by moving.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must happen for a depositary to be free from liability after committing transgression?
A) He must compensate the owner
B) He must remove the transgression and return the deposit to its proper state
C) He must obtain the owner's forgiveness
D) He must never commit transgression again

Key Takeaway
Removing a transgression restores the depositary's trust status and eliminates liability for subsequent loss.

Mixing Deposit with Own Property: Liability and Partnership
Ruling on mixing deposit with own property and conditions of indistinguishability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on mixing a deposit with the depositary's own property and distinguish between cases that lead to liability and those that create a partnership.

Core Idea
Mixing without permission leads to liability only if the mixture is indistinguishable; otherwise the owner's right remains intact.

When a depositary mixes the entrusted property with his own, the outcome depends critically on two factors: permission and distinguishability. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi position, clarifying that liability arises only when the mixture is practically indistinguishable and the mixing was done without permission. If the items remain easily separable or if the mixing was consensual, a partnership of ownership results without liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the depositary did (spending part of the deposit, then mixing its like with the rest) and the consequence (liable for the whole).
The act is unauthorized mixing after returning a similar amount.
2
Locate the condition of indistinguishability
Note that the ruling is restricted to cases where the mixture is 'not distinguishable' (la tata mayyazu). If easily separable (e.g., walnuts and almonds, black and white dirhams), the owner's right is not cut off by consensus.
Indistinguishability here means practical ease of separation, not absolute impossibility.
3
Distinguish between mixing with and without permission
If the depositary mixes with the owner's permission, it becomes a partnership (sharika). If mixing occurs without his action (e.g., accidental), also a partnership without liability.
Permission or lack of action negates liability.
4
Determine consequences for perishing
In a partnership created by mixing without liability, if some property perishes, it is considered from both proportionally. The remainder is divided according to each party's original share.
This mirrors general rules of joint ownership (shirkat al-milk).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوديعة اشتركا ولو أنفق بعضها فرد مثله فخلطه بالباقي ضمن الكل وإن تعدى فيها ثم أزال صغيراً كان أو كبيراً، ولا يضمن أبوه لأجله ; كذا في الخلاصة. وقيد بكونها لا تتميز لأنه لو كان يمكن الوصول إليه على وجه التيسير كخلط الجوز باللوز والدراهم السود بالبيض فإنه لا ينقطع حق المالك إجماعاً، واستفيذ منه أن المراد بعدم التميز عدمه على وجه التيسير لا عدم إمكانه مطلقاً كما لا يخفى وإن خلطها بإذنه كان شريكاً له قوله : (وإن اختلط بغير فعله اشتركا) يعني وكانت شركة ملك ولا ضمان عليه لعدم الصنع منه فإن هلك بعضها هلك من مالهما جميعاً ويقسم الباقي بينهما على قدر ما كان لكل واحد منهما كالمال المشترك
The book of deposit: They both become partners, and if he spent some of it and returned its like and mixed it with the rest, he guarantees the whole, and if he transgressed in it then removed the transgression, be it small or large, his father does not guarantee for him; thus in al-Khulasa. And it is restricted to it being not distinguishable because if it was possible to reach it easily, like mixing walnuts with almonds and black dirhams with white, then the right of the owner is not cut off by consensus. And from this it is understood that the intent of not being distinguishable is not being distinguishable easily, not absolute impossibility, as is not hidden. And if he mixed it with his permission, he becomes a partner with him. His saying: (And if it becomes mixed without his action, they both become partners) meaning it is a partnership of ownership and no liability upon him due to the lack of action from him. So if some of it perishes, it perishes from the wealth of both, and the remainder is divided between them according to what each one had, like joint property.

Commentary
This passage presents rulings on mixing a deposit with the depositary's own property. It begins by stating the general principle: if the depositary spends part of the deposit, returns its like (mithl), and mixes it with the rest, he is liable for the whole. If he later removes the transgression, liability ceases. The passage then specifies that the ruling of mixing applies only when the mixture is indistinguishable (la tata mayyazu). If the items could be easily distinguished (e.g., walnuts mixed with almonds, or black dirhams with white), the owner's right is not cut off by consensus. The commentator clarifies that the condition of indistinguishability refers to practical ease of separation, not absolute impossibility. If the depositary mixed with the owner's permission, it becomes a partnership (sharika). Then the matn states: 'If it becomes mixed without his action, they both become partners.' This means a partnership of ownership arises, and the depositary is not liable because he did not cause the mixing. If some of the mixed property perishes, it is considered from both, and the remainder is divided proportionally.

Key Terms
خَلْط (khalṭ): Mixing the deposit with the depositary's own property without permission leading to liability if indistinguishable.
اشتراك (ishtirāk): Partnership in ownership when mixing occurs by mutual consent or without action of depositary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a depositary mixes the deposited dirhams with his own white dirhams, and the mixture is easily distinguishable (e.g., by color), what is the ruling?
A) The depositary is liable for all the mixed dirhams.
B) The owner's right is not cut off, and the depositary is not liable for the whole.
C) The mixing is void and the deposit is returned as is.
D) The depositary becomes a partner with the owner.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on mixing deposits hinges on practical indistinguishability: without permission, it triggers liability; with permission or without action, it creates a partnership.

Spending Part of Deposit and Returning Its Like: When Does Liability Attach?
Ruling with reasoning on depositary's liability when spending and returning like.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on a depositary who spends part of a deposit and returns its like, identifying when full liability attaches and when it does not.

Core Idea
Returning a like (mithl) does not clear liability; only returning the exact item (ʿayn) restores the trust.

Imagine you entrust your money to someone for safekeeping, and they use a portion for themselves, later returning a similar amount. Does that clear them? The Hanafi school draws a sharp distinction: returning a like (mithl) introduces their own property, creating a mixed trust. This lesson unpacks the precise conditions under which the depositary becomes liable for the entire deposit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The depositary spends part of the deposit, returns a like (mithl) in kind and quantity, and mixes it with the remaining deposit. The ruling is that he guarantees the whole (daman al-kull).
This is the primary scenario discussed in the passage.
2
Analyze the dual basis for liability
Liability arises from two acts: spending (ta'addi/transgression) and mixing. Spending is a breach of trust. Mixing his own property with the deposit makes the mixture indistinguishable, so the trust is not restored.
The mixing must be such that it cannot be easily differentiated.
3
Distinguish the exception
If the depositary marks his own property (e.g., places a distinguishing mark) so that it can be identified, he is liable only for the amount he spent, not the entire deposit. The mixing here is not considered indistinguishable.
This exception shows that the key factor is whether the mixture can be separated.
4
Compare with returning the actual item (ʿayn)
If the depositary took part of the deposit, then returned the exact same item (not a like) to its original place, no liability arises even if the deposit is later lost. The trust is restored because the specific property is returned.
This distinction between ʿayn and mithl is central to Hanafi deposit law.
5
Extend the principle to sale
The passage analogizes to a sale: if the depositary sells the deposit and guarantees its value, ownership relates back to the moment of liability. This reinforces that the critical moment is when the trust is breached and not restored by returning a like.
This analogy strengthens the rule against returning mithl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو أنفق بعضها فرد مثله فخلطه بالباقي ضمن الكل أي البعض بالإنفاق والبعض بالخلط لأنه متعد بالإنفاق منها ورد مثله باقٍ على ملكه وقد خلطه بما بقي من الوديعة فضمن الجميع . والمراد بالخلط هنا خلط لا تتميز معه، أما لو جعل عل ماله علامة حين خلطه بها بحيث يتأتى التمييز لا يضمن إلا ما أنفق؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وقيد بالإنفاق ورد المثل لأنه إذا أخذ بعض الوديعة لينفقه في حاجته فرده إلى موضعه ثم ضاعت الوديعة فلا ضمان عليه لوجهين : الأول أن رفعه حفظ فلا يضمن به ولا بمجرد النية الثاني أنه وإن صار ضامناً بالرفع فقد عاد إلى الوفاق برد العين إلى مكانها فبرئ عن الضمان بخلاف ما إذا رد مثله لأنه إنما جاء بملك نفسه فلا يكون عوداً إلى الوفاق وهو أولى من الأول، فإنهم قالوا بأنه لو باعها وضمن قيمتها نفذ البيع من جهته واستند ملكه بالضمان إلى وقت وجوب الضمان فلو لم يكن الرفع للبيع موجباً للضمان عليه قبل البيع والتسليم لم يستند ملكه إلى تلك الحالة؛ كذا في النهاية. وقيد بقوله «فرد مثلها لأنه لو لم يرد كان ضامناً لما أنفق خاصة لأنه حافظ للباقي ولم يتعيب لأنه مما لا يضره التبعيض لأن الكلام فيما إذا كانت الوديعة دراهم أو دنانير أو أشياء من المكيل والموزون فهو كما لو أودعه وديعتين فأنفق إحداهما لا يكون ضامناً للأخرى؛ كذا في النهاية
His saying: (And if he spent some of it and returned its like and mixed it with the rest, he guarantees the whole) meaning he guarantees part due to spending and part due to mixing, because he transgressed by spending from it and he returned its like which remains his property, and he mixed it with what remained of the deposit, so he guarantees all of it. What is meant by mixing here is mixing that does not allow differentiation. But if he placed a mark on his property when mixing it such that differentiation is possible, he only guarantees what he spent; thus in al-Khulasa. And it is restricted by spending and returning the like because if he took some of the deposit to spend on his need and returned it to its place, then the deposit was lost, there is no liability on him for two reasons: first, his taking was for safekeeping so he is not liable by it nor by mere intention; second, even if he became liable by the taking, he has returned to compliance by returning the actual item to its place, so he is freed from liability, unlike the case where he returned its like, because he brought his own property and that is not a return to compliance. And this is more appropriate than the first, for they said that if he sold it and guaranteed its value, the sale is executed from his side and his ownership by guarantee relates back to the time liability became due; so if the taking for sale did not create liability before sale and delivery, his ownership would not relate back to that state; thus in al-Nihaya. And it is restricted by his saying 'returned its like' because if he did not return, he is only liable for what he spent specifically, because he is the custodian of the rest and it is not defective because partial loss does not harm it, as the discussion is about deposits of dirhams or dinars or things of measured or weighed items; so it is like if he was given two deposits and he spent one, he is not liable for the other; thus in al-Nihaya.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a depositary spends part of the deposit and then returns an equivalent amount (mithl) and mixes it with the remaining deposit. The commentary clarifies that liability arises for two reasons: the spending (which is a transgression) and the mixing (which makes the property indistinguishable). The mixing here refers to a mixture that cannot be easily distinguished; if the depositary marks his own property so it can be differentiated, he is only liable for what he spent. The commentator then distinguishes two scenarios: (1) if the depositary takes some deposit for his need, uses it, and returns the same item (ayn) to its place, then no liability arises because taking for safekeeping is not a transgression, and returning the actual item restores the trust. (2) If he returns a like (mithl) instead of the very item, he remains liable because he mixed his own property with the deposit. The passage also compares with sale: if he sells the deposit and guarantees its value, the sale is valid and ownership relates back. Finally, if he does not return anything, he is only liable for what he spent, not the rest, because the deposit is of a type that is not harmed by partial use (like dirhams or wheat).

Key Terms
مِثْل (mithl): An equivalent item (like kind and quantity) returned by depositary instead of the original taken deposit.
عَيْن (ʿayn): The specific physical item that was deposited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A depositary takes 10 dirhams from a deposit of 50 dirhams, returns 10 other dirhams of his own, and mixes them with the remaining 40 dirhams. What is the ruling?
A) He is liable only for the 10 dirhams he took.
B) He is not liable because he returned the same amount.
C) He is liable for the entire 50 dirhams because he returned a like and mixed indistinguishably.
D) He becomes a partner with the owner.

Key Takeaway
Returning a like (mithl) and mixing it indistinguishably with the remaining deposit makes the depositary liable for the entire deposit; only returning the exact item (ʿayn) restores the trust.

Removal of Transgression and Distinction Between Depository, Borrower, and Lessee
Sharah expansion of a matn rule on liability after transgression

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on removal of transgression, identify the condition for liability cessation, and distinguish the cases of depositary, borrower, and lessee, including the exception for the borrower for pawn.

Core Idea
Removing transgression restores trust for a depositary, but not for a borrower or lessee—unless the borrower’s act fulfills the owner’s purpose.

This lesson unpacks a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh regarding liability after transgression (taʿaddī) in trust (amāna) contracts. While a depositary (mūdiʿ) can shed liability by returning to compliance, a borrower (mustaʿīr) and lessee (mustaʾjir) cannot—because their possession is for their own benefit. An important exception is the borrower who borrows to pawn an item; his delivery to the pledgee is treated as constructive return to the owner. Understanding these nuances prevents misapplication of liability rules in everyday contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Extract the main ruling from the matn: if a depositary transgresses (e.g., rides, wears, employs the deposit) and then removes the transgression (returns the item to his custody), his liability (ḍamān) ceases.
The removal of transgression must be actual return to the depositary's hand.
2
Add the Condition from the Commentary
Note the condition from al-Ẓāhiriyya: the depositary must not intend to return to the transgression. If he removes the garment at night intending to wear it by day, and it is stolen at night, liability remains.
Intention to repeat the transgression prevents the cessation of liability.
3
Contrast with Borrower/Lessee
Understand that for a borrower or lessee, even if they remove the transgression, liability does not cease. Their hand is for themselves; return to compliance does not restore the owner's trust because they act for their own benefit.
The depositary's hand is like the owner's hand; borrower/lessee's hand is for themselves.
4
Apply the Exception for Borrower to Pawn
Study the exception from al-Mabsūṭ: a borrower who borrows an item to pawn it and transgresses before pawning, then pawns it, is free from liability if the item perishes after the debt is paid. Delivery to the pledgee constitutes constructive return to the owner because it fulfills the lender's purpose.
This exception treats the borrower for pawn like a depositary due to the fulfillment of the owner's objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن تعدى فيها ثم أزال التعدي زال الضمان) أي تعدي في الوديعة بأن كانت دابة فركبها أو ثوباً فلبسه أو عبداً فاستخدمه أو أودعها غيره ثم أزال التعدي فردها إلى يده برئ عن الضمان لأنه مأمور بالحفظ في كل الأوقات، فإذا خالف في البعض ثم رجع أتى بالمأمور به كما إذا استأجره للحفظ شهراً فترك الحفظ في بعضه ثم حفظ في الباقي استحق الأجرة بقدره، وقد قدمنا في باب الجنايات على الإحرام عن الظهيرية أنه يزول الضمان عنه بشرط أنه لا على العود إلى التعدي حتى لو نزع ثوب الوديعة ليلاً ومن عزمه أن يلبسه نهاراً ثم سرق ليلاً لا يبرأ . عن الضمان فراجعه . قوله : (بخلاف المستعير والمستأجر) إذا تعديا ثم أزالاه لا يزول الضمان لأن البراءة عنه إنما تكون بالإعادة إلى يد المالك حقيقة أو تقديراً ويدهما لهما لأنهما عاملان لأنفسهما بخلاف المودع فإن يده كيد المالك. ويستثنى من إطلاق المصنف تبعاً لغيره من استعار شيئاً ليرهنه فتعدى فيه كما إذا استعار عبداً ليرهنه أو دابة فاستخدم العبد وركب الدابة قبل أن يرهنهما ثم رهنهما بمال بمثل قيمتهما ثم قضى المال ولم يقبضهما حتى هلكت عند المرتهن لا ضمان على الراهن لأنه قد برئ عن الضمان حين رهنهما، فإن كان أميناً خالف فقد عاد إلى الوفاق. وإنما كان مستعير الرهن كالمودع لأن تسليمه إلى المرتهن يرجع إلى تحقيق مقصود المعير حتى لو هلك بعد ذلك يصير دينه مقضياً فيستوجب المعير الرجوع على الراهن بمثله فكان ذلك بمنزلة الرد عليه حكماً فلهذا برئ من الضمان؛ كذا في المبسوط من باب الإعارة في الرهن
His saying: (And if he transgressed in it then removed the transgression, the liability ceases) i.e., he transgressed in the deposit, such as if it was an animal and he rode it, or a garment and he wore it, or a slave and he employed him, or he deposited it with someone else, then he removed the transgression and returned it to his hand, he is free from liability because he is commanded to safeguard at all times. So if he violated in some part then returned, he has done what he was commanded, just as if he was hired for safeguarding for a month and he abandoned safeguarding in some of it then safeguarded in the rest, he deserves the wage proportionally. And we have presented in the chapter of crimes against ihram from al-Zahiriyyah that liability ceases from him on condition that he does not intend to return to transgression, such that if he removed the deposit garment at night intending to wear it during the day, and it was stolen at night, he is not free from liability; so refer to it. His saying: (Unlike the borrower and the lessee) if they transgress and then remove the transgression, liability does not cease because absolution from it only occurs by return to the hand of the owner actually or constructively, and their hand is for themselves because they act for themselves, unlike the depositary whose hand is like the hand of the owner. And there is an exception to the general statement of the author following others: one who borrows something to pawn it and then transgresses in it, such as if he borrowed a slave to pawn or an animal, and used the slave and rode the animal before pawning them, then pawned them for property equal to their value, then paid off the debt and did not take them back until they perished in the possession of the pledgee, there is no liability on the pawner because he became free from liability at the time of pawning them. For he was a trustee, he transgressed, but then returned to compliance. And the borrower for pawn is like the depositary because his delivery to the pledgee realizes the purpose of the lender, so that if it perishes after that, his debt becomes discharged, so the lender is entitled to recourse against the borrower for its like; thus it is like constructive return to him, and therefore he is free from liability; thus in al-Mabsut from the chapter of borrowing in pledge.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage addresses the removal of transgression (izalat al-ta'di) by a depositary. The matn states that if the depositary transgresses (e.g., rides the deposited animal, wears the garment, employs the slave, or re-deposits with another) and then removes the transgression by returning the item to his own custody, liability ceases. The commentator explains that the depositary is commanded to safeguard at all times; if he violates that command but then returns to compliance, he is like an employee hired for a month who abandons work for part of it but works the rest—he deserves proportional wage. However, a condition is added: the depositary must not intend to return to transgression. If he removes a garment at night intending to wear it by day and it gets stolen at night, he is not free from liability. Then the matn distinguishes the borrower (musta'ir) and lessee (mustajir): even if they remove transgression, liability remains because their hand is for themselves (acting for their own benefit), unlike the depositary whose hand is like the owner's hand (acting for the owner's benefit). An exception is noted: if someone borrows an item specifically to pawn it (e.g., a slave to pawn, or an animal to pawn), and before pawning he uses it (rides or employs), then pawns it for its value, pays off the debt, and the item perishes with the pledgee before retrieval—there is no liability on the borrower-pawner because at the time of pawning he returned to compliance and the delivery to the pledgee fulfills the lender's purpose, constituting a constructive return to the owner.

Key Terms
تَعَدٍّ (taʿaddī): Transgression by using the deposit in a way not permitted, such as riding, wearing, employing, or re-depositing.
مُسْتَعِير (mustaʿīr): One who borrows an item for use; his possession is for his own benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following statements is correct regarding the borrower for pawn (musta'ir li al-rahn)?
A) His liability never ceases.
B) He is treated like a depositary because delivery to the pledgee fulfills the owner's purpose.
C) He is treated like an ordinary borrower because he acts for himself.
D) His liability ceases only if the item is returned to the owner.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a depositary can eliminate liability by removing transgression (without intent to repeat), but a borrower or lessee cannot; an exception exists for the borrower for pawn, whose delivery to the pledgee counts as constructive return to the owner.

Confession after Denial: Why It Does Not Remove Liability
Ruling with conditions and qiyas on depositary's confession after denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a depositary's denial (jahd) incurs liability and explain why subsequent confession (iqrar) does not remove it.

Core Idea
Denial rescinds the deposit contract; confession cannot restore a rescinded contract.

In this passage, the commentator explains that if a depositary denies the deposit under specific conditions, the contract is terminated and the depositary becomes liable. Even if he later confesses, liability remains because the contract can only be renewed by a new agreement. This ruling is analogized to an agent denying agency or a seller denying the sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the act
The ruling is that confession after denial does not remove liability (daman). The act is the depositary's denial (jahd) followed by confession (iqrar).
Denial alone does not always incur liability; it must meet conditions.
2
List the conditions for liability upon denial
The text specifies five conditions: (1) denial of the deposit itself (not a claim of gift/sale), (2) denial in the owner's presence, (3) after the owner demands return, (4) the depositary moves the deposit from its place, and (5) the deposit is movable. If any condition is missing, no liability.
Real estate is not liable upon denial according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
3
Understand the talil (legal reasoning)
Denial rescinds the contract by qiyas to denial of agency and denial of sale. Once rescinded, the contract cannot be revived except by a new contract. Confession is not a new contract; it is just a statement.
The analogy is based on the common illa of 'rescission of the contract'.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on real estate
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that denial of real estate does not incur liability because real estate cannot be moved. Muhammad holds that if the real estate is movable in nature (e.g., crops), denial does incur liability even if not physically moved.
This ikhtilaf affects the practical application for deposits like land with crops.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإقراره بعد جحوده) يعني أن المودع إذا جحد الوديعة بأن قال لم يودعني عند مالكها بعد طلب ردها ونقلها من مكانها وقت الإنكار وكانت منقولاً ولم يكن هناك من يخاف منه عليها ولم يحضرها بعد الجحود لمالكها ثم أقر بها لا يزول الضمان لأن الجحود رفع للعقد فيفسخ به العقد فلا يعود إلا بعقد جديد كجحود الوكيل الوكالة وجحود أحد المتبايعين البيع. قيدنا بكونه أنكر الإيداع لأن المودع لو ادعى أن المالك وهبها منه أو باعها له وأنكر صاحبها ثم هلكت لا ضمان على المودع ; كذا في الخلاصة وقيدنا بيكون الإنكار عند المالك جحودها عند غيره لا يوجب الضمان وقيدنا بكونه بعد الطلب لأنه لو قال له ما حال عندك ليشكر على حفظها فجحدها لا ضمان عليه وقيدنا بكونه نقلها لأنه لو لم ينقلها من مكانها حال جحوده فهلكت لا ضمان عليه ; كذا في الخلاصة عن الأجناس . وقيدنا بكونه منقولاً لأنها لو كانت عقاراً لا يضمن بالجحود عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد لأن المنقول : إذا كانت الوديعة أو العارية مما يحول يضمن بالجحود وإن لم يحولها نور العين قوله : (وإن أقام البينة أنه ردها قبل الجحود الخ رأيت ملحقاً في نسختي الخلاصة بعد لفظة الجحود قبلت بينته وبعده كلمة ممحوة لم أعرفها. وفي الخانية وذكر في المنتفى إذا جحد المودع الوديعة ثم ادعى أنه ردها بعد ذلك وأقام البينة قبلت بينته وكذا لو أقام البينة أنه ردها قبل الجحود وقال إنما غلطت الخ. فظهر أن فيما نقله المؤلف سقطاً. وفي الخانية أيضاً : ولو جحد المودع الوديعة ثم أقام البينة على هلاكها قبل الجحود إن قال ليس لك عندي وديعة قبلت بينته ويبرأ عن الضمان، ولو قال نسيت في الجحود أو قال غلطت ثم أقام البينة أنه دفعها إلى صاحبها قبل الجحود برئ.
His saying: (And his confession after his denial) means that if the depositary denies the deposit by saying 'He did not deposit with me', after the owner demands its return and the depositary moves it from its place at the time of denial, it being movable, and there is no one to fear from it, and he does not present it to its owner after denial, then he confesses, liability does not cease because denial cancels the contract, so the contract is rescinded and does not return except by a new contract, like the denial of agency by the agent and the denial of sale by one of the two contracting parties. We restrict it to his denial of the deposit because if the depositary claimed that the owner gave it to him as a gift or sold it to him and the owner denied that, then it perishes, there is no liability on the depositary; thus in al-Khulasa. And we restrict it to the denial being in the presence of the owner; denial in front of others does not incur liability. And we restrict it to being after the demand because if he said to him 'What is with you? may Allah reward you for safeguarding it' and he denied, there is no liability. And we restrict it to his moving it because if he did not move it from its place at the time of denial, and it perishes, there is no liability; thus in al-Khulasa from al-Ajnas. And we restrict it to it being movable because if it was real estate, he is not liable by denial according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad, because the movable: if the deposit or the borrowed item is something that can be moved, he is liable by denial even if he did not move it (Nur al-Ayn). His saying: (And if he adduces evidence that he returned it before the denial... I saw an appendix in my copy of al-Khulasa after the word 'denial' that 'his evidence is accepted', and after it a word erased that I did not recognize. And in al-Khaniya: and it is mentioned in al-Muntafa that if the depositary denies the deposit then claims that he returned it after that and adduces evidence, his evidence is accepted; and likewise if he adduces evidence that he returned it before the denial and said 'I merely made a mistake'... So it appears that there is an omission in what the author transmitted. And in al-Khaniya also: if the depositary denies the deposit then adduces evidence of its loss before the denial, if he said 'You have no deposit with me', his evidence is accepted and he is free from liability; and if he said 'I forgot' in the denial or said 'I made a mistake', then adduces evidence that he delivered it to its owner before the denial, he is free from liability.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage deals with the situation where a depositary initially denies the deposit (jahd) but later confesses (iqrar). The matn states that confession after denial does not remove liability. The commentator explains that denial amounts to terminating the contract (raf' al-'aqd), and the contract can only be restored by a new contract, similar to an agent denying agency or one party denying a sale. Several conditions are required for the denial to cause liability: (1) the denial must be of the deposit itself (not a claim of gift or sale by the depositary), (2) the denial must be made in front of the owner, (3) it must be after the owner demands return, (4) the depositary must have moved the deposit from its place during denial, and (5) the deposit must be movable (not real estate). According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, denial of real estate does not incur liability, while Muhammad holds that it does if it is something that can be moved (even if not physically moved). The passage then discusses evidence of return before denial. The commentator notes a missing word in his copy of al-Khulasa but cites al-Khaniya and al-Muntafa: if the depositary denies and then adduces evidence that he returned the deposit after the denial or that he made a mistake, his evidence is accepted and he is freed from liability. Similarly, if he denies and then proves that the deposit perished before the denial, he is freed.

Key Terms
جَحْد (jahd): Denial of the deposit by the depositary, which rescinds the contract and incurs liability under conditions.
إقرار (iqrār): Confession by the depositary after denial; does not restore the contract or remove liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for a depositary's denial to incur liability?
A) The denial is made in the owner's presence.
B) The deposit is movable.
C) The depositary returns the deposit before confession.
D) The depositary moves the deposit from its place.

Key Takeaway
A depositary's denial (jahd) under specific conditions rescinds the contract and creates liability that confession (iqrar) cannot undo.

Evidence of Return Before Denial: When the Depositary Is Freed
Ruling on acceptance of evidence of return after denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a depositary's evidence of return before denial is accepted and identify the scenarios where this applies.

Core Idea
Evidence of return before denial is accepted, even after a false denial, because the denial may have been a mistake.

The passage discusses that if a depositary denies the deposit but later produces evidence that he had returned it before the denial, his evidence is accepted and he is freed from liability. This applies whether he admits his earlier denial was a mistake or not. The commentator notes some textual ambiguities but clarifies the ruling from other sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the ruling
The depositary denies the deposit, then produces evidence that he returned it before the denial. The ruling is that his evidence is accepted and he is freed from liability.
This applies even if he says 'I made a mistake' regarding the denial.
2
Understand the rationale
The denial may have been made in error or forgetfulness. Evidence of return before denial proves that the deposit was already discharged, so the depositary should not be liable.
The ruling is from al-Khaniya and al-Muntafa, overcoming the initial denial.
3
Note the additional scenarios
If the depositary adduces evidence of return after the denial, it is also accepted. Similarly, if he adduces evidence of loss of the deposit before the denial, he is freed.
The text mentions an omission in the transmitted matn regarding the word after 'denial'.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, or documentary proof.

What is required for a depositary to be freed after a denial and subsequent evidence of loss?
A) The evidence must show loss occurred after the denial.

Travelling with a Deposit: Conditions and Liability
Commentary on the depositary's right to travel with the deposit and related liabilities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a depositary may travel with the deposit and apply the Hanafi rules on liability when conditions are breached.

Core Idea
The depositary may travel with the deposit unless prohibited or fearful — the command to preserve is absolute.

In Hanafi fiqh, a deposit (wadi'ah) is a non-binding contract for safekeeping. The depositary is entrusted with the property, but may he travel with it? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, establishing the default permission and the conditions that trigger liability. You will learn the underlying legal reasoning (dalil) and the intra-madhhab differences among the Hanafi imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Begin by reading the passage's first line: 'And he may travel with it if there is no prohibition and no fear'. This is the default permission (mubah).
The default is permission, not restriction.
2
Extract the conditions for liability
The passage specifies two limitations: if the depositor forbade travel, or if there is fear for the deposit's safety. If either exists and the depositary travels, he becomes liable (daman).
Fear is objective (e.g., dangerous road, sea travel).
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The commentator argues: 'because the command is absolute, so it is not restricted by place just as it is not restricted by time.' This is a qiyas (analogy) from the unrestricted time dimension to the place dimension.
The command to preserve is general, implying spatial freedom.
4
Understand the scope: items with carriage and expense
The author's absolute wording includes items with or without carriage/mu'nah. However, later authorities (e.g., Shaykh Abu Nasr) make an exception for large quantities of food by istihsan.
Light items are easier to transport; istihsan considers custom.
5
Contrast with the agent (wakil) ruling
The passage ends by comparing the depositary to a sales agent. An agent who travels with the goods becomes liable if the agency was location-restricted or if the goods are heavy and he had an alternative.
This highlights the specificity of the deposit ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وله أن يسافر بها عند عدم النهي (والخوف أي للمودع أن يسافر بالوديعة إذا لم ينهه المودع ولم يخف عليها بالإخراج لأن الأمر مطلق فلا يتقيد بالمكان كما لا يتقيد بالزمان . قيد بعدم النهي لأنه لو نهاه عن السفر ليس له ذلك، وقيد بعدم الخوف لأن الطريق لو كان مخيفاً وله بد من السفر كان ضامناً، وكذا الأب الوصي وإن لم يكن له بد منه إن سافر بأهله، اه. وإن سافر بنفسه يكون ضامناً ؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان. ومن المخوف السفر في البحر لأن الغالب فيه العطب ؛ كذا في الاختيار. وأطلق المصنف فشمل ماله حمل ومؤنة طال الخروج أو قصر وهو قول الإمام ؛ كذا في النهاية. واستثنى منه الشيخ أبو نصر في شرح القدوري الطعام الكثير فإنه يضمن إذا سافر به استحساناً. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : وللمودع أن يسافر بمال الوديعة عندنا إذا لم يكن لها حمل ومؤنة. وقيد بالوديعة لأن الوكيل بالبيع إذا سافر بما وكل ببيعه إن قيد الوكالة بمكان بأن قال بعه بالكوفة فأخرجها من الكوفة يصير ضامناً عندنا ، وإن أطلق الوكالة فسافر به إن كان شيئاً له حمل ومؤنة يكون ضامناً، وإن لم يكن له حمل ومؤنة لا ضامناً عندنا إذا لم يكن له بد من السفر، وإن كان له بد من السفر لا يصير يكون ضامناً عند أبي حنيفة طال الخروج أم قصر . وقال محمد يكون ضامناً طال الخروج أم قصر. وقال أبو يوسف: إن طال الخروج يكون ضامناً، وإن قصر لا يكون ضامناً؛ كذا في فتاوی قاضیخان
His statement: (And he may travel with it if there is no prohibition and no fear) meaning the depositary may travel with the deposit if the depositor did not forbid him and he does not fear for it due to removal, because the command is absolute, so it is not restricted by place just as it is not restricted by time. He restricted by the absence of prohibition because if the depositor forbade him from travelling, he does not have that right. And he restricted by the absence of fear because if the road is frightening and he has an alternative to travel, he is liable. Likewise, the father or guardian, even if he has no alternative, if he travels with his family... (end of quote). And if he travels himself, he becomes liable; as in Fatawa Qadikhan. Among frightening things is travel by sea because destruction is prevalent; as in al-Ikhtiyar. The author made it absolute, so it includes items with carriage and expense, whether the journey is long or short, and it is the statement of Imam; as in al-Nihaya. Al-Shaykh Abu Nasr in Sharh al-Quduri excluded large quantities of food, for which he is liable if he travels, by istihsan. In Fatawa Qadikhan: The depositary may travel with the deposit in our school if it does not have carriage and expense. He restricted to deposit because an agent for sale, if he travels with what he was appointed to sell, if the agency is restricted to a place, e.g., 'sell it in Kufa' and he takes it out of Kufa, he becomes liable in our school. If the agency is absolute, then if he travels with it and it has carriage and expense, he is liable; if it does not have carriage and expense, he is not liable in our school if he has no alternative to travel. If he has an alternative to travel, he does not become liable according to Abu Hanifa whether the journey is long or short. Muhammad said he becomes liable whether long or short. Abu Yusuf said: if the journey is long, he becomes liable; if short, he does not become liable; as in Fatawa Qadikhan.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the rule from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the depositary travelling with the deposit. The default ruling is that the depositary has permission to travel with the deposit because the deposit contract gives unrestricted custody. However, two conditions must be met: (1) the depositor did not explicitly prohibit travel, and (2) there is no genuine fear for the safety of the deposit, e.g., if the route is dangerous and the depositary has an alternative (like staying or taking a safe route), he becomes liable if he travels anyway. The commentary then discusses extensions: the same rule applies to a father or guardian travelling with orphan property? Even if unavoidable, they are liable if they travel with their own family. Additional rulings from Fatawa Qadikhan clarify that travel by sea is inherently fearful. The author (Kanz) made the rule absolute, covering items with or without significant carriage? Actually the later part indicates that if the item has significant carriage and expense (heavy or burdensome), the depositary is liable when travelling? The text reconciles: if the item is light and has no carriage, the depositary is not liable. This is the relied-upon position of the school. The passage ends by contrasting the depositary with a sales agent (wakil bil-bay'), for whom liability rules differ: if the agency is restricted to a location, travelling outside makes him liable; if unrestricted, liability depends on the item's burden and the necessity of travel. This shows the specificity of the deposit ruling.

Key Terms
وديعة (wadi'ah): a deposit: property left with another for safekeeping without compensation.
ضمان (daman): liability to compensate for loss or damage due to breach of duty.
خوف (khawf): fear for the safety of the deposit due to external dangers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the depositary become liable if he travels with the deposit?
A) If he travels despite the depositor's explicit prohibition.
B) If he travels by sea regardless of conditions.
C) If the deposit has no carriage and expense.
D) If he travels during the night.

Key Takeaway
The depositary may travel with the deposit unless the depositor forbade it or there is objective fear; in those cases, liability attaches.

Depositary's Denial and Later Claim: Liability and Valuation
Ruling on the depositary's denial and later claim of return or loss

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a depositary who denies the deposit is not believed in a later claim of return or loss, and determine the valuation date for compensation.

Core Idea
Denial of a trust makes the depositary liable; later claims are rejected unless corroborated.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq resolves a textual discrepancy in al-Khulasa regarding the depositary who first denies the deposit and later claims it was returned or lost. The core rule is that such denial prevents the depositary from being believed in his later defense, and it triggers liability for the deposit's value. We will walk through the ruling, the proof from established practice, and the subtle point about how to value the deposit when the denial date value is unknown.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the depositary's denial of the deposit (juhud). The ruling is that after such denial, he is not credited when he later claims return or loss. This is the default position from al-Khulasa.
2
Understand the reason for the ruling
The denial itself is a contradiction to any later claim of safekeeping, return, or loss. The law treats this as a breach of trust, making the depositary liable whether or not actual loss occurred.
Compare: if he had not denied, he might be believed with an oath.
3
Recognize the valuation nuance
Once liability is established, the next question is the deposit's value for compensation. The sources (e.g., al-Imadiyya) state: value on the day of denial if known; if unknown, value on the day of deposit. This resolves an apparent omission in some texts.
The value at denial reflects the moment the trust was breached.
4
Verify the textual resolution
The commentator shows that the original wording of al-Khulasa actually agrees with the valuation rule, despite a misquotation. The correct rule is consistent across works.
This demonstrates how later commentators (shuruh) reconcile apparent conflicts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثم ادعى الرد أو الهلاك لا يصدق عبارة الخلاصة بعد قوله لم تستودعني هكذا: وفي الأقضية لو قال لم يستودعني ثم ادعى الرد أو الهلاك لا يصدق ففي عبارته سقط قوله: (ويدل عليه ما ذكره في الخلاصة (الخ) قال في المنح : لكن ذكر في العمادية أنه لو جحد الوديعة وهلكت ثم أقام المودع بينة على قيمتها يوم الجحود يقضى بقيمتها يوم الجحود، وإن لم يعلم قيمتها . يوم الجحود يقضى يوم الإيداع يعني إذا أثبت الوديعة كذا ذكر في العدة وتمام هذا ينظر في وديعة الذخيرة اهـ. وكتب بعض الفضلاء على هامش المنح أن فيما نقل من عبارة الخلاصة سقطاً وأن أصل العبارة موافق لما في العمادية لأن أصل العبارة قضى عليه بقيمته يوم الجحود، فإن قال الشهود لا نعلم قيمته يوم الجحود لكن قيمته يوم الإيداع كذا قضى عليه بقيمته يوم الإيداع .
His statement: (Then he claimed return or loss, he is not believed) – the wording of al-Khulasa after his statement 'you did not entrust me' is as follows: In al-Aqdiya, if he said 'he did not entrust me' then claimed return or loss, he is not believed. So in his wording there is an omission. (And it is indicated by what is mentioned in al-Khulasa...) Said in al-Minh: But it is mentioned in al-Imadiyya that if he denied the deposit and it perished, then the depositor established evidence of its value on the day of denial, judgment is given for its value on the day of denial; and if its value on the day of denial is not known, judgment is given for the value on the day of deposit, meaning if he proves the deposit. Similarly mentioned in al-Idda; and the rest of this is looked up in Wadi'at al-Dhakhira. End. And some virtuous wrote on the margin of al-Minh that there is an omission in what is transmitted from the wording of al-Khulasa, and that the original wording agrees with what is in al-Imadiyya, because the original wording: he is judged by its value on the day of denial; if the witnesses say 'we do not know its value on the day of denial but its value on the day of deposit is such', he is judged by its value on the day of deposit.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage addresses the situación where a depositary first denies the deposit ('you never entrusted me with anything') and later claims that he returned it or it was lost. The ruling from al-Khulasa is that he is not believed in his later claim because his initial denial contradicts it. However, a textual discrepancy is noted: some sources (e.g., al-Imadiyya) suggest that if the deposit perished after denial, and the depositor proves the value on the day of denial, judgment is based on that value; if that value is unknown, the value on the day of deposit is used. The commentator resolves the apparent contradiction by showing that the original text of al-Khulasa actually agrees: judgment is on the day of denial, but if witnesses don't know that value, the day of deposit is used. This illustrates the Hanafi rule that a person who denies a trust (or debt) and then later admits or claims a defense is generally not believed without corroboration, but once he becomes liable (e.g., due to denial causing loss), the valuation date is contested. The relied-upon position, as per 'Idda and other works, is that the value is assessed on the day of denial if known, otherwise on the day of deposit.

Key Terms
جحود (juhud): denial of a right or trust by the custodian.
قيمة (qimah): market value of the item at a specific date.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a depositary denies the deposit and then claims it was lost, what is the default ruling?
A) He is believed because loss is possible
B) He is not believed because his denial contradicts the claim
C) He is believed only if he provides witnesses
D) He is believed if the value is high

Key Takeaway
A depositary who denies the deposit is not believed in later claims of return or loss; he becomes liable for the value assessed on the day of denial if known, otherwise on the day of deposit.
